      <p><b>Guatemala</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span><span title="'w' in 'wind'">w</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Guatemala.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20"></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e4/En-us-Guatemala.ogg" class="internal" title="En-us-Guatemala.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">GWAH</span>-tə-<span style="font-size:90%">MAH</span>-lə</i></a>; <small>Spanish:&nbsp;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Spanish" title="Help:IPA/Spanish">[ɡwateˈmala]</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:ES-pe_-_Guatemala.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20"></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3b/ES-pe_-_Guatemala.ogg" class="internal" title="ES-pe - Guatemala.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span>), officially the <b>Republic of Guatemala</b> (Spanish: <i lang="es">República de Guatemala</i>), is a country in <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a> bordered by <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> to the north and west, <a href="/wiki/Belize" title="Belize">Belize</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Sea" title="Caribbean Sea">Caribbean</a> to the northeast, <a href="/wiki/Honduras" title="Honduras">Honduras</a> to the east, <a href="/wiki/El_Salvador" title="El Salvador">El Salvador</a> to the southeast and the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a> to the south. With an estimated population of around 17.2 million,<sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_2019_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP_2019-8">[8]</a></sup> it is the most populous country in Central America and has the 11th largest national population in the Americas. Guatemala is a <a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">representative democracy</a>; its capital and largest city is Nueva Guatemala de la Asunción, also known as <a href="/wiki/Guatemala_City" title="Guatemala City">Guatemala City</a>. </p>
      <p>The core of the <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Maya civilization</a>, which extended across <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a>, was historically based in the territory of modern Guatemala. In the 16th century, most of this area was <a href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_Guatemala" title="Spanish conquest of Guatemala">conquered by the Spanish</a> and claimed as part of the <a href="/wiki/Viceroyalty" title="Viceroyalty">viceroyalty</a> of <a href="/wiki/New_Spain" title="New Spain">New Spain</a>. Guatemala attained independence in 1821 as part of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Republic_of_Central_America" title="Federal Republic of Central America">Federal Republic of Central America</a>, which dissolved by 1841. </p>
      <p>From the mid- to late 19th century, Guatemala suffered chronic instability and civil strife. Beginning in the early 20th century, it was ruled by a series of dictators backed by the <a href="/wiki/United_Fruit_Company" title="United Fruit Company">United Fruit Company</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">government</a>. In 1944, the authoritarian leader <a href="/wiki/Jorge_Ubico" title="Jorge Ubico">Jorge Ubico</a> was overthrown by a pro-democratic military coup, initiating a <a href="/wiki/Guatemalan_Revolution" title="Guatemalan Revolution">decade-long revolution</a> that led to sweeping social and economic reforms. A <a href="/wiki/1954_Guatemalan_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1954 Guatemalan coup d'état">U.S.-backed military coup</a> in 1954 ended the revolution and installed a dictatorship.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlakeley200992_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlakeley200992-9">[9]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>From 1960 to 1996, Guatemala <a href="/wiki/Guatemalan_Civil_War" title="Guatemalan Civil War">endured a bloody civil war</a> fought between the US-backed government and <a href="/wiki/Left-wing_politics" title="Left-wing politics">leftist</a> rebels, including <a href="/wiki/Guatemalan_genocide" title="Guatemalan genocide">genocidal massacres</a> of the Maya population perpetrated by the military.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2008171_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2008171-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolano20123–15_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolano20123–15-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENavarro1999_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENavarro1999-12">[12]</a></sup> Since a <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>-negotiated peace accord, Guatemala has achieved both economic growth and successful democratic elections, although it continues to struggle with high rates of poverty, crime, drug trade, and instability. As of 2014<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Guatemala ranks 31st of 33 Latin American and Caribbean countries in terms of the <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Guatemala's abundance of biologically significant and unique ecosystems includes many endemic species and contributes to Mesoamerica's designation as a <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_hotspot" title="Biodiversity hotspot">biodiversity hotspot</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConservation_International2007_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConservation_International2007-14">[14]</a></sup> </p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>The name "Guatemala" comes from the <a href="/wiki/Nahuatl_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Nahuatl language">Nahuatl</a> word <i>Cuauhtēmallān</i> <a href="https://nah.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cuauht%C4%93mall%C4%81n" class="extiw" title="nah:Cuauhtēmallān">(nahwiki)</a>, or "place of many trees", a derivative of the <a href="/wiki/K%27iche%27_people" class="mw-redirect" title="K'iche' people">K'iche' Mayan</a> word for "many trees"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell1997_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell1997-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETroika_study_abroad_programs2006_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETroika_study_abroad_programs2006-16">[16]</a></sup> or, perhaps more specifically, for the Cuate/Cuatli tree <a href="/wiki/Eysenhardtia" title="Eysenhardtia">Eysenhardtia</a>. This was the name that the <a href="/wiki/Tlaxcala" title="Tlaxcala">Tlaxcaltecan</a> warriors who accompanied <a href="/wiki/Pedro_de_Alvarado" title="Pedro de Alvarado">Pedro de Alvarado</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Conquest" class="mw-redirect" title="Spanish Conquest">Spanish Conquest</a> gave to this territory.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERain_Forest_Wordpress2013_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERain_Forest_Wordpress2013-17">[17]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Guatemala" title="History of Guatemala">History of Guatemala</a>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Columbian">Pre-Columbian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Pre-Columbian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>The first evidence of human habitation in Guatemala dates to 12,000 BC. Archeological evidence, such as <a href="/wiki/Obsidian" title="Obsidian">obsidian</a> <a href="/wiki/Arrowhead" title="Arrowhead">arrowheads</a> found in various parts of the country, suggests a human presence as early as 18,000 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> There is archaeological proof that early Guatemalan settlers were <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherers" class="mw-redirect" title="Hunter-gatherers">hunter-gatherers</a>. Pollen samples from <a href="/wiki/Pet%C3%A9n_Basin" title="Petén Basin">Petén</a> and the Pacific coast indicate that <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a> cultivation had been developed by the people by 3500 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Sites dating to 6500 BC have been found in the <a href="/wiki/Quich%C3%A9_department" class="mw-redirect" title="Quiché department">Quiché</a> region in the Highlands, and <a href="/wiki/Sipacate" title="Sipacate">Sipacate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Escuintla" title="Escuintla">Escuintla</a> on the central Pacific coast. </p>
      <p>Archaeologists divide the <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_era" title="Pre-Columbian era">pre-Columbian</a> history of Mesoamerica into the Preclassic period (2999 BC to 250 AD), the Classic period (250 to 900 AD), and the Postclassic period (900 to 1500 AD).<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Until recently, the Preclassic was regarded by researchers as a formative period, in which the peoples typically lived in huts in small villages of farmers, with few permanent buildings. </p>
      <p>This notion has been challenged since the late 20th century by discoveries of monumental architecture from that period, such as an altar in <a href="/wiki/La_Blanca" title="La Blanca">La Blanca</a>, <a href="/wiki/San_Marcos_department" class="mw-redirect" title="San Marcos department">San Marcos</a>, from 1000 BC; ceremonial sites at Miraflores and <a href="/wiki/Naranjo" title="Naranjo">Naranjo</a> from 801 BC; the earliest monumental masks; and the <a href="/wiki/Mirador_Basin" title="Mirador Basin">Mirador Basin</a> cities of <a href="/wiki/Nakb%C3%A9" class="mw-redirect" title="Nakbé">Nakbé</a>, Xulnal, <a href="/wiki/El_Tintal" title="El Tintal">El Tintal</a>, Wakná and <a href="/wiki/El_Mirador" title="El Mirador">El Mirador</a>. </p>
      <p>On 3 June 2020, researchers published an article in <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i> describing their discovery of the oldest and largest Maya site, known as <a href="/wiki/Aguada_F%C3%A9nix" title="Aguada Fénix">Aguada Fénix</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>. It features monumental architecture, an elevated, rectangular plateau measuring about 1,400 meters long and nearly 400 meters wide, constructed of a mixture of earth and clay. To the west is a 10-meter-tall earthen mound. Remains of other structures and reservoirs were also detected through the <a href="/wiki/Lidar" title="Lidar">Lidar</a> technology. It is estimated to have been built from 1000 to 800 BC, demonstrating that the Maya built large, monumental complexes from their early period.<sup id="cite_ref-bower_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bower-21">[21]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Tikal_mayan_ruins_2009.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Tikal_mayan_ruins_2009.jpg/300px-Tikal_mayan_ruins_2009.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Tikal_mayan_ruins_2009.jpg/450px-Tikal_mayan_ruins_2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Tikal_mayan_ruins_2009.jpg/600px-Tikal_mayan_ruins_2009.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4288" data-file-height="2472"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Tikal_mayan_ruins_2009.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Maya city of <a href="/wiki/Tikal" title="Tikal">Tikal</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The Classic period of <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican" class="mw-redirect" title="Mesoamerican">Mesoamerican</a> civilization corresponds to the height of the <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Maya civilization</a>. It is represented by countless sites throughout Guatemala, although the largest concentration is in <a href="/wiki/Pet%C3%A9n_Basin" title="Petén Basin">Petén</a>. This period is characterized by urbanisation, the emergence of independent city-states, and contact with other Mesoamerican cultures.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>This lasted until approximately 900 AD, when the <a href="/wiki/Classic_Maya_collapse" title="Classic Maya collapse">Classic Maya civilization collapsed</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gill_2000_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gill_2000-23">[23]</a></sup> The Maya abandoned many of the cities of the central lowlands or were killed by a drought-induced <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gill_2000_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gill_2000-23">[23]</a></sup> The cause of the collapse is debated, but the drought theory is gaining currency, supported by evidence such as lakebeds, ancient pollen, and others.<sup id="cite_ref-Gill_2000_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gill_2000-23">[23]</a></sup> A series of prolonged droughts in what is otherwise a seasonal desert is thought to have decimated the Maya, who relied on regular rainfall to support their dense population.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoster2000_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoster2000-24">[24]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Post-Classic period is represented by regional kingdoms, such as the <a href="/wiki/Itza_people" title="Itza people">Itza</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kowoj" title="Kowoj">Kowoj</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yalain" title="Yalain">Yalain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kejache" title="Kejache">Kejache</a> in Petén, and the <a href="/wiki/Mam_people" title="Mam people">Mam</a>, <a href="/wiki/K%27iche%27_people" class="mw-redirect" title="K'iche' people">Ki'che'</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kaqchikel_people" title="Kaqchikel people">Kackchiquel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chajoma" title="Chajoma">Chajoma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tz%27utujil_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Tz'utujil people">Tz'utujil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poqomchi%27_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Poqomchi' people">Poqomchi'</a>, <a href="/wiki/Q%27eqchi%27_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Q'eqchi' people">Q'eqchi'</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ch%27orti%27_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Ch'orti' people">Ch'orti'</a> peoples in the highlands. Their cities preserved many aspects of Maya culture. </p>
      <p>The Maya civilization shares many features with other Mesoamerican civilizations due to the high degree of interaction and <a href="/wiki/Cultural_diffusion" class="mw-redirect" title="Cultural diffusion">cultural diffusion</a> that characterized the region. Advances such as writing, <a href="/wiki/Epigraphy" title="Epigraphy">epigraphy</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Maya_calendar" title="Maya calendar">calendar</a> did not originate with the Maya; however, their civilization fully developed them. Maya influence can be detected from <a href="/wiki/Honduras" title="Honduras">Honduras</a>, Guatemala, and Northern <a href="/wiki/El_Salvador" title="El Salvador">El Salvador</a> to as far north as central Mexico, more than 1,000&nbsp;km (620&nbsp;mi) from the <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Mesoamerica#Maya_Region" title="Geography of Mesoamerica">Maya area</a>. Many outside influences are found in <a href="/wiki/Maya_art" class="mw-redirect" title="Maya art">Maya art</a> and architecture, which are thought to have resulted from trade and cultural exchange rather than direct external conquest. </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Archaeological_investigation">Archaeological investigation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Archaeological investigation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>In 2018, 60,000 uncharted structures were revealed in northern Guatemala by archaeologists with the help of <a href="/wiki/Lidar" title="Lidar">Lidar</a> technology lasers. The project applied Lidar technology on an area of 2,100 square kilometers in the <a href="/wiki/Maya_Biosphere_Reserve" title="Maya Biosphere Reserve">Maya Biosphere Reserve</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Pet%C3%A9n_Department" title="Petén Department">Petén</a> region of Guatemala. Thanks to the new findings, archaeologists believe that 7-11 million <a href="/wiki/Maya_peoples" title="Maya peoples">Maya people</a> inhabited northern Guatemala during the late classical period from 650 to 800 A.D., twice the estimated population of medieval England.<sup id="cite_ref-NatlGeo_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NatlGeo-25">[25]</a></sup> Lidar technology digitally removed the tree canopy to reveal ancient remains and showed that Maya cities, such as <a href="/wiki/Tikal" title="Tikal">Tikal</a>, were larger than previously assumed. The use of Lidar revealed numerous houses, palaces, elevated highways, and defensive fortifications. According to archaeologist Stephen Houston, it is one of the most overwhelming findings in over 150 years of Maya archaeology.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NatlGeo_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NatlGeo-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Colonial_era_.281519.E2.80.931821.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial_era_(1519–1821)">Colonial era (1519–1821)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Colonial era (1519–1821)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_Guatemala" title="Spanish conquest of Guatemala">Spanish conquest of Guatemala</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_Pet%C3%A9n" title="Spanish conquest of Petén">Spanish conquest of Petén</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Pedro_de_Alvarado_(Tom%C3%A1s_Povedano).jpg" class="image"><img alt="Painting of a bearded man in early 16th-century attire including prominent ruff collar, wearing a decorative breastplate, with his right hand resting on his hip and his left hand grasping a cane, or riding crop." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Pedro_de_Alvarado_%28Tom%C3%A1s_Povedano%29.jpg/170px-Pedro_de_Alvarado_%28Tom%C3%A1s_Povedano%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="237" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Pedro_de_Alvarado_%28Tom%C3%A1s_Povedano%29.jpg/255px-Pedro_de_Alvarado_%28Tom%C3%A1s_Povedano%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Pedro_de_Alvarado_%28Tom%C3%A1s_Povedano%29.jpg/340px-Pedro_de_Alvarado_%28Tom%C3%A1s_Povedano%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2302" data-file-height="3211"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Pedro_de_Alvarado_(Tom%C3%A1s_Povedano).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <i>Conquistador</i> <a href="/wiki/Pedro_de_Alvarado" title="Pedro de Alvarado">Pedro de Alvarado</a> led the initial Spanish efforts to conquer Guatemala.<sup id="cite_ref-Lovell05p58_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lovell05p58-36">[36]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>After they arrived in the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>, the Spanish started several expeditions to Guatemala, beginning in 1519. Before long, Spanish contact resulted in an epidemic that devastated native populations. <a href="/wiki/Hern%C3%A1n_Cort%C3%A9s" title="Hernán Cortés">Hernán Cortés</a>, who had led the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_Mexico" class="mw-redirect" title="Spanish conquest of Mexico">Spanish conquest of Mexico</a>, granted a permit to Captains Gonzalo de Alvarado and his brother, <a href="/wiki/Pedro_de_Alvarado" title="Pedro de Alvarado">Pedro de Alvarado</a>, to conquer this land. Alvarado at first allied himself with the <a href="/wiki/Kaqchikel_people" title="Kaqchikel people">Kaqchikel nation</a> to fight against their traditional rivals the <a href="/wiki/K%27iche%27_kingdom_of_Q%27umarkaj" class="mw-redirect" title="K'iche' kingdom of Q'umarkaj">K'iche' (Quiché) nation</a>. Alvarado later turned against the Kaqchikel, and eventually brought the entire region under Spanish domination.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During the colonial period, Guatemala was an <a href="/wiki/Audiencia_Real" class="mw-redirect" title="Audiencia Real">audiencia</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Captaincy" title="Captaincy">captaincy-general</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Captaincy_General_of_Guatemala" title="Captaincy General of Guatemala">Capitanía General de Guatemala</a></i>) of Spain, and a part of <a href="/wiki/New_Spain" title="New Spain">New Spain</a> (Mexico).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoster200069–71_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoster200069–71-38">[38]</a></sup> The first capital, Villa de Santiago de Guatemala (now known as <a href="/wiki/Tecpan_Guatemala" class="mw-redirect" title="Tecpan Guatemala">Tecpan Guatemala</a>), was founded on 25 July 1524 near <a href="/wiki/Iximch%C3%A9" class="mw-redirect" title="Iximché">Iximché</a>, the Kaqchikel capital city. The capital was moved to <a href="/wiki/Ciudad_Vieja" title="Ciudad Vieja">Ciudad Vieja</a> on 22 November 1527, as a result of a Kaqchikel attack on Villa de Santiago de Guatemala. </p>
      <p>On 11 September 1541, the new capital was flooded when the lagoon in the <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_crater" title="Volcanic crater">crater</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Volc%C3%A1n_de_Agua" title="Volcán de Agua">Agua Volcano</a> collapsed due to heavy rains and earthquakes; the capital was then moved 6&nbsp;km (4&nbsp;mi) to <a href="/wiki/Antigua_Guatemala" title="Antigua Guatemala">Antigua</a> in the Panchoy Valley, now a <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a>. This city was destroyed by several earthquakes in 1773–1774. The King of Spain authorized moving the capital to its current location in the Ermita Valley, which is named after a <a href="/wiki/Catholic_church" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic church">Catholic church</a> dedicated to the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="Virgin Mary">Virgen del Carmen</a>. This new capital was founded on 2 January 1776. </p> 
      <h3><span id="Independence_and_the_19th_century_.281821.E2.80.931847.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence_and_the_19th_century_(1821–1847)">Independence and the 19th century (1821–1847)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Independence and the 19th century (1821–1847)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Independenciacentroamerica2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Independenciacentroamerica2.jpg/220px-Independenciacentroamerica2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="130" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Independenciacentroamerica2.jpg/330px-Independenciacentroamerica2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Independenciacentroamerica2.jpg/440px-Independenciacentroamerica2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="789" data-file-height="467"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Independenciacentroamerica2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Criollo_people" title="Criollo people">Criollos</a> rejoice upon learning about the declaration of independence from Spain on 15 September 1821.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>On 15 September 1821, the <a href="/wiki/Captaincy_General_of_Guatemala" title="Captaincy General of Guatemala">Captaincy General of Guatemala</a>, an administrative region of the Spanish Empire consisting of <a href="/wiki/Chiapas" title="Chiapas">Chiapas</a>, Guatemala, <a href="/wiki/El_Salvador" title="El Salvador">El Salvador</a>, Nicaragua, Costa Rica, and Honduras, officially proclaimed its independence from Spain. Independence from Spain was gained, and the Captaincy General of Guatemala joined the <a href="/wiki/First_Mexican_Empire" title="First Mexican Empire">First Mexican Empire</a> under <a href="/wiki/Agust%C3%ADn_de_Iturbide" title="Agustín de Iturbide">Agustin de Itubide</a>. </p>
      <p>Under the First Empire, Mexico reached its greatest territorial extent, stretching from northern&nbsp;California&nbsp;to the provinces of&nbsp;Central America&nbsp;(excluding&nbsp;Panama, which was then part of&nbsp;Colombia), which had not initially approved becoming part of the Mexican Empire but joined the Empire shortly after their independence. This region was formally a part of the <a href="/wiki/New_Spain" title="New Spain">Viceroyalty of New Spain</a> throughout the colonial period, but as a practical matter had been administered separately. It was not until 1825 that Guatemala created its own flag.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1838 the liberal forces of Honduran leader <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Moraz%C3%A1n" title="Francisco Morazán">Francisco Morazán</a> and Guatemalan <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Francisco_Barrundia" title="José Francisco Barrundia">José Francisco Barrundia</a> invaded Guatemala and reached San Sur, where they executed Chúa Alvarez, father-in-law of <a href="/wiki/Rafael_Carrera" title="Rafael Carrera">Rafael Carrera</a>, then a military commander and later the first president of Guatemala. The liberal forces impaled Alvarez's head on a pike as a warning to followers of the Guatemalan <a href="/wiki/Caudillo" title="Caudillo">caudillo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison200884-85_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison200884-85-40">[40]</a></sup> Carrera and his wife Petrona – who had come to confront Morazán as soon as they learned of the invasion and were in <a href="/wiki/Mataquescuintla" title="Mataquescuintla">Mataquescuintla</a> – swore they would never forgive Morazán even in his grave; they felt it impossible to respect anyone who would not avenge family members.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison200885_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison200885-41">[41]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>After sending several envoys, whom Carrera would not receive – and especially not Barrundia whom Carrera did not want to murder in cold blood – Morazán began a scorched-earth offensive, destroying villages in his path and stripping them of assets. The Carrera forces had to hide in the mountains.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison200886_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison200886-42">[42]</a></sup> Believing Carrera totally defeated, Morazán and Barrundia marched to <a href="/wiki/Guatemala_City" title="Guatemala City">Guatemala City</a>, and were welcomed as saviors by state governor Pedro Valenzuela and members of the conservative <a href="/w/index.php?title=Aycinena_clan&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Aycinena clan (page does not exist)">Aycinena clan</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&nbsp;[<a href="https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan_Aycinena" class="extiw" title="es:Clan Aycinena">es</a>]</span>, who proposed to sponsor one of the liberal battalions, while Valenzuela and Barrundia gave Morazán all the Guatemalan resources needed to solve any financial problem he had.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison200887_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison200887-43">[43]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Criollo_people" title="Criollo people">criollos</a> of both parties celebrated until dawn that they finally had a criollo caudillo like Morazán, who was able to crush the peasant rebellion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison200888_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison200888-44">[44]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Political_Evolution_of_Central_America_and_the_Caribbean_1830_na.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Political_Evolution_of_Central_America_and_the_Caribbean_1830_na.png/220px-Political_Evolution_of_Central_America_and_the_Caribbean_1830_na.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Political_Evolution_of_Central_America_and_the_Caribbean_1830_na.png/330px-Political_Evolution_of_Central_America_and_the_Caribbean_1830_na.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Political_Evolution_of_Central_America_and_the_Caribbean_1830_na.png/440px-Political_Evolution_of_Central_America_and_the_Caribbean_1830_na.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="750"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Political_Evolution_of_Central_America_and_the_Caribbean_1830_na.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Federal_Republic_of_Central_America" title="Federal Republic of Central America">Federal Republic of Central America</a> (1823–1838) with its capital in Guatemala City.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Morazán used the proceeds to support Los Altos and then replaced Valenzuela with <a href="/wiki/Mariano_Rivera_Paz" title="Mariano Rivera Paz">Mariano Rivera Paz</a>, a member of the Aycinena clan, although he did not return to that clan any property confiscated in 1829. In revenge, <a href="/wiki/Juan_Jos%C3%A9_de_Aycinena_y_Pi%C3%B1ol" title="Juan José de Aycinena y Piñol">Juan José de Aycinena y Piñol</a> voted to dissolve the <a href="/wiki/Central_American_Federation" class="mw-redirect" title="Central American Federation">Central American Federation</a> in <a href="/wiki/San_Salvador" title="San Salvador">San Salvador</a> a little later, forcing Morazán to return to El Salvador to fight for his federal mandate. Along the way, Morazán increased repression in eastern Guatemala, as punishment for helping Carrera.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison200889_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison200889-45">[45]</a></sup> Knowing that Morazán had gone to El Salvador, Carrera tried to take <a href="/wiki/Salam%C3%A1" title="Salamá">Salamá</a> with the small force that remained, but was defeated, and lost his brother Laureano in combat. With just a few men left, he managed to escape, badly wounded, to <a href="/wiki/Sanarate" title="Sanarate">Sanarate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison200891-92_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison200891-92-46">[46]</a></sup> After recovering somewhat, he attacked a detachment in <a href="/wiki/Jutiapa" title="Jutiapa">Jutiapa</a> and got a small amount of booty which he gave to the volunteers who accompanied him. He then prepared to attack <a href="/wiki/Petapa" class="mw-redirect" title="Petapa">Petapa</a> near Guatemala City, where he was victorious, although with heavy casualties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison200892_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison200892-47">[47]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In September of that year, Carrera attempted an assault on the capital of Guatemala, but the liberal general <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Salazar_Castro" title="Carlos Salazar Castro">Carlos Salazar Castro</a> defeated him in the fields of <a href="/wiki/Villa_Nueva,_Guatemala" title="Villa Nueva, Guatemala">Villa Nueva</a> and Carrera had to retreat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHernández_de_León195920_April_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHernández_de_León195920_April-48">[48]</a></sup> After unsuccessfully trying to take <a href="/wiki/Quetzaltenango" title="Quetzaltenango">Quetzaltenango</a>, Carrera found himself both surrounded and wounded. He had to capitulate to Mexican General <a href="/wiki/Agustin_Guzman" class="mw-redirect" title="Agustin Guzman">Agustin Guzman</a>, who had been in Quetzaltenango since <a href="/wiki/Vicente_Fil%C3%ADsola" class="mw-redirect" title="Vicente Filísola">Vicente Filísola</a>'s arrival in 1823. Morazán had the opportunity to shoot Carrera, but did not, because he needed the support of the Guatemalan peasants to counter the attacks of <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Ferrera" title="Francisco Ferrera">Francisco Ferrera</a> in <a href="/wiki/El_Salvador" title="El Salvador">El Salvador</a>. Instead, Morazán left Carrera in charge of a small fort in Mita, without any weapons. Knowing that Morazán was going to attack El Salvador, <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Ferrera" title="Francisco Ferrera">Francisco Ferrera</a> gave arms and ammunition to Carrera and convinced him to attack Guatemala City.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison200896_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison200896-49">[49]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Meanwhile, despite insistent advice to definitively crush Carrera and his forces, Salazar tried to negotiate with him diplomatically; he even went as far as to show that he neither feared nor distrusted Carrera by removing the fortifications of the Guatemalan capital, in place since the battle of Villa Nueva.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHernández_de_León195920_April_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHernández_de_León195920_April-48">[48]</a></sup> Taking advantage of Salazar's good faith and Ferrera's weapons, Carrera took Guatemala City by surprise on 13 April 1839; Salazar, <a href="/wiki/Mariano_G%C3%A1lvez" title="Mariano Gálvez">Mariano Gálvez</a> and Barrundia fled before the arrival of Carrera's militiamen. Salazar, in his nightshirt, vaulted roofs of neighboring houses and sought refuge,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHernández_de_León195948_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHernández_de_León195948-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008122–127_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008122–127-51">[51]</a></sup> reaching the border disguised as a peasant.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHernández_de_León195948_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHernández_de_León195948-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008122–127_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008122–127-51">[51]</a></sup> With Salazar gone, Carrera reinstated Rivera Paz as head of state. </p>
      <p>Between 1838 and 1840 a <a href="/wiki/Los_Altos,_Central_America" class="mw-redirect" title="Los Altos, Central America">secessionist movement</a> in the city of <a href="/wiki/Quetzaltenango" title="Quetzaltenango">Quetzaltenango</a> founded the breakaway state of <a href="/wiki/Los_Altos_(country)" class="mw-redirect" title="Los Altos (country)">Los Altos</a> and sought independence from Guatemala. The most important members of the Liberal Party of Guatemala and liberal enemies of the conservative régime moved to Los Altos, leaving their exile in El Salvador.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHernández_de_León1959?_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHernández_de_León1959?-52">[52]</a></sup> The liberals in Los Altos began severely criticizing the Conservative government of Rivera Paz.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHernández_de_León1959?_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHernández_de_León1959?-52">[52]</a></sup> Los Altos was the region with the main production and economic activity of the former state of Guatemala. Without Los Altos, conservatives lost many of the resources that had given Guatemala hegemony in Central America.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHernández_de_León1959?_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHernández_de_León1959?-52">[52]</a></sup> The government of Guatemala tried to reach a peaceful solution, but two years of bloody conflict followed. </p>
      <p>In 1840, Belgium began to act as an external source of support for Carrera's independence movement, in an effort to exert influence in Central America. The <i>Compagnie belge de colonisation</i> (Belgian Colonization Company), commissioned by Belgian <a href="/wiki/Leopold_I_of_Belgium" title="Leopold I of Belgium">King Leopold I</a>, became the administrator of <a href="/wiki/Santo_Tomas_de_Castilla" class="mw-redirect" title="Santo Tomas de Castilla">Santo Tomas de Castilla</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECompagnie_Belge_de_Colonisation1844_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECompagnie_Belge_de_Colonisation1844-53">[53]</a></sup> replacing the failed British <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Coast_of_Central_America_Commercial_and_Agricultural_Company" title="Eastern Coast of Central America Commercial and Agricultural Company">Eastern Coast of Central America Commercial and Agricultural Company</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECompagnie_Belge_de_Colonisation1844_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECompagnie_Belge_de_Colonisation1844-53">[53]</a></sup> Even though the colony eventually crumbled, Belgium continued to support Carrera in the mid-19th century, although Britain continued to be the main business and political partner to Carrera.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodward1993498_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodward1993498-54">[54]</a></sup> Rafael Carrera was elected Guatemalan Governor in 1844. </p>
      <p><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_Guatemalans&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="German Guatemalans (page does not exist)">Settlers from Germany</a> arrived in the mid-19th century. German settlers acquired land and grew coffee plantations in Alta Verapaz and Quetzaltenango.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Republic_.281847.E2.80.931851.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Republic_(1847–1851)">Republic (1847–1851)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Republic (1847–1851)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>On 21 March 1847, Guatemala declared itself an independent republic and Carrera became its first president. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Guatemala_Real_1847_Silver_Coin_Fonrobert_7236.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Guatemala_Real_1847_Silver_Coin_Fonrobert_7236.jpg/220px-Guatemala_Real_1847_Silver_Coin_Fonrobert_7236.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="107" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Guatemala_Real_1847_Silver_Coin_Fonrobert_7236.jpg/330px-Guatemala_Real_1847_Silver_Coin_Fonrobert_7236.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Guatemala_Real_1847_Silver_Coin_Fonrobert_7236.jpg/440px-Guatemala_Real_1847_Silver_Coin_Fonrobert_7236.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1827" data-file-height="892"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Guatemala_Real_1847_Silver_Coin_Fonrobert_7236.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Proclamation Coin 1847 of the independent Republic of Guatemala
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>During the first term as president, Carrera brought the country back from extreme conservatism to a traditional moderation; in 1848, the liberals were able to drive him from office, after the country had been in turmoil for several months.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHernández_de_León1930_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHernández_de_León1930-55">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiceli197472_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiceli197472-56">[56]</a></sup> Carrera resigned of his own free will and left for México. The new liberal regime allied itself with the Aycinena family and swiftly passed a law ordering Carrera's execution if he returned to Guatemalan soil.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHernández_de_León1930_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHernández_de_León1930-55">[55]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The liberal criollos from <a href="/wiki/Quetzaltenango" title="Quetzaltenango">Quetzaltenango</a> were led by general <a href="/wiki/Agust%C3%ADn_Guzm%C3%A1n" title="Agustín Guzmán">Agustín Guzmán</a> who occupied the city after Corregidor general <a href="/wiki/Mariano_Paredes_(Guatemala)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mariano Paredes (Guatemala)">Mariano Paredes</a> was called to <a href="/wiki/Guatemala_City" title="Guatemala City">Guatemala City</a> to take over the presidential office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008270_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008270-57">[57]</a></sup> They declared on 26 August 1848 that Los Altos was an independent state once again. The new state had the support of <a href="/wiki/Doroteo_Vasconcelos" title="Doroteo Vasconcelos">Doroteo Vasconcelos</a>' régime in <a href="/wiki/El_Salvador" title="El Salvador">El Salvador</a> and the rebel guerrilla army of Vicente and Serapio Cruz, who were sworn enemies of Carrera.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008270–271_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008270–271-58">[58]</a></sup> The interim government was led by Guzmán himself and had Florencio Molina and the priest Fernando Davila as his Cabinet members.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008271_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008271-59">[59]</a></sup> On 5 September 1848, the criollos altenses chose a formal government led by Fernando Antonio Martínez. </p>
      <p>In the meantime, Carrera decided to return to Guatemala and did so, entering at <a href="/wiki/Huehuetenango" title="Huehuetenango">Huehuetenango</a>, where he met with native leaders and told them that they must remain united to prevail; the leaders agreed and slowly the segregated native communities started developing a new Indian identity under Carrera's leadership.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008275_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008275-60">[60]</a></sup> In the meantime, in the eastern part of Guatemala, the <a href="/wiki/Jalapa" class="mw-redirect" title="Jalapa">Jalapa</a> region became increasingly dangerous; former president <a href="/wiki/Mariano_Rivera_Paz" title="Mariano Rivera Paz">Mariano Rivera Paz</a> and rebel leader Vicente Cruz were both murdered there after trying to take over the Corregidor office in 1849.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008275_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008275-60">[60]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>When Carrera arrived to <a href="/wiki/Chiantla" title="Chiantla">Chiantla</a> in <a href="/wiki/Huehuetenango" title="Huehuetenango">Huehuetenango</a>, he received two altenses emissaries who told him that their soldiers were not going to fight his forces because that would lead to a native revolt, much like that of 1840; their only request from Carrera was to keep the natives under control.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008275_60-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008275-60">[60]</a></sup> The altenses did not comply, and led by Guzmán and his forces, they started chasing Carrera; the caudillo hid, helped by his native allies and remained under their protection when the forces of <a href="/wiki/Miguel_Garcia_Granados" class="mw-redirect" title="Miguel Garcia Granados">Miguel Garcia Granados</a> arrived from <a href="/wiki/Guatemala_City" title="Guatemala City">Guatemala City</a> looking for him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008275_60-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008275-60">[60]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On learning that officer <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_V%C3%ADctor_Zavala" title="José Víctor Zavala">José Víctor Zavala</a> had been appointed as Corregidor in Suchitepéquez, Carrera and his hundred <a href="/wiki/Jakaltek_people" title="Jakaltek people">jacalteco</a> bodyguards crossed a dangerous jungle infested with <a href="/wiki/Jaguar" title="Jaguar">jaguars</a> to meet his former friend. Zavala not only did not capture him, he agreed to serve under his orders, thus sending a strong message to both liberal and conservatives in Guatemala City that they would have to negotiate with Carrera or battle on two fronts – Quetzaltenango and Jalapa.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008278_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008278-61">[61]</a></sup> Carrera went back to the Quetzaltenango area, while Zavala remained in Suchitepéquez as a tactical maneuver.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008-62">[62]</a></sup> Carrera received a visit from a cabinet member of Paredes and told him that he had control of the native population and that he assured Paredes that he would keep them appeased.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008278_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008278-61">[61]</a></sup> When the emissary returned to Guatemala City, he told the president everything Carrera said, and added that the native forces were formidable.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008279_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008279-63">[63]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Guzmán went to <a href="/wiki/Antigua,_Guatemala" class="mw-redirect" title="Antigua, Guatemala">Antigua</a> to meet with another group of Paredes emissaries; they agreed that Los Altos would rejoin Guatemala, and that the latter would help Guzmán defeat his enemy and also build a port on the Pacific Ocean.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008279_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008279-63">[63]</a></sup> Guzmán was sure of victory this time, but his plan evaporated when in his absence Carrera and his native allies occupied Quetzaltenango; Carrera appointed Ignacio Yrigoyen as <a href="/wiki/Corregidor_(position)" title="Corregidor (position)">Corregidor</a> and convinced him that he should work with the K'iche', Q'anjobal and <a href="/wiki/Mam_people" title="Mam people">Mam</a> leaders to keep the region under control.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008280_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008280-64">[64]</a></sup> On his way out, Yrigoyen murmured to a friend: "Now he is the king of the Indians, indeed!"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008280_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008280-64">[64]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Guzmán then left for Jalapa, where he struck a deal with the rebels, while <a href="/wiki/Luis_Batres_Juarros" title="Luis Batres Juarros">Luis Batres Juarros</a> convinced President Paredes to deal with Carrera. Back in Guatemala City within a few months, Carrera was commander-in-chief, backed by military and political support of the Indian communities from the densely populated western highlands.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeaver1999138_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeaver1999138-65">[65]</a></sup> During the first presidency, from 1844 to 1848, he brought the country back from excessive conservatism to a moderate regime, and – with the advice of Juan José de Aycinena y Piñol and <a href="/wiki/Pedro_de_Aycinena" class="mw-redirect" title="Pedro de Aycinena">Pedro de Aycinena</a> – restored relations with the Church in Rome with a <a href="/wiki/Concordat_of_1854" title="Concordat of 1854">Concordat</a> ratified in 1854.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalvert198536_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalvert198536-66">[66]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Second_Carrera_government_.281851.E2.80.931865.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_Carrera_government_(1851–1865)">Second Carrera government (1851–1865)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Second Carrera government (1851–1865)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Carrerayturcios_2014-06-22_09-46.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Carrerayturcios_2014-06-22_09-46.jpg/170px-Carrerayturcios_2014-06-22_09-46.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Carrerayturcios_2014-06-22_09-46.jpg/255px-Carrerayturcios_2014-06-22_09-46.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Carrerayturcios_2014-06-22_09-46.jpg/340px-Carrerayturcios_2014-06-22_09-46.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="2048"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Carrerayturcios_2014-06-22_09-46.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Captain General <a href="/wiki/Rafael_Carrera" title="Rafael Carrera">Rafael Carrera</a> after being appointed President for Life in 1854.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>After Carrera returned from exile in 1849 the president of El Salvador, <a href="/wiki/Doroteo_Vasconcelos" title="Doroteo Vasconcelos">Doroteo Vasconcelos</a>, granted asylum to the Guatemalan liberals, who harassed the Guatemalan government in several different ways. José Francisco Barrundia established a liberal newspaper for that specific purpose. Vasconcelos supported a rebel faction named "La Montaña" in eastern Guatemala, providing and distributing money and weapons. By late 1850, Vasconcelos was getting impatient at the slow progress of the war with Guatemala and decided to plan an open attack. Under that circumstance, the Salvadorean head of state started a campaign against the conservative Guatemalan regime, inviting <a href="/wiki/Honduras" title="Honduras">Honduras</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a> to participate in the alliance; only the <a href="/wiki/Honduras" title="Honduras">Honduran</a> government led by <a href="/wiki/Juan_Lindo" title="Juan Lindo">Juan Lindo</a> accepted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHernández_de_León1930_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHernández_de_León1930-55">[55]</a></sup> In 1851 Guatemala defeated an Allied army from Honduras and El Salvador at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_La_Arada" title="Battle of La Arada">Battle of La Arada</a>. </p>
      <p>In 1854 Carrera was declared "supreme and perpetual leader of the nation" for life, with the power to choose his successor. He held that position until he died on 14 April 1865. While he pursued some measures to set up a foundation for economic prosperity to please the conservative landowners, military challenges at home and a three-year war with Honduras, El Salvador, and Nicaragua dominated his presidency. </p>
      <p>His rivalry with Gerardo Barrios, President of El Salvador, resulted in open war in 1863. At <a href="/wiki/Coatepeque,_Quetzaltenango" title="Coatepeque, Quetzaltenango">Coatepeque</a> the Guatemalans suffered a <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Coatepeque" title="Battle of Coatepeque">severe defeat</a>, which was followed by a truce. Honduras joined with El Salvador, and Nicaragua and Costa Rica with Guatemala. The contest was finally settled in favor of Carrera, who besieged and occupied <a href="/wiki/San_Salvador" title="San Salvador">San Salvador</a>, and dominated Honduras and Nicaragua. He continued to act in concert with the Clerical Party, and tried to maintain friendly relations with European governments. Before he died, Carrera nominated his friend and loyal soldier, Army Marshall <a href="/wiki/Vicente_Cerna_y_Cerna" title="Vicente Cerna y Cerna">Vicente Cerna y Cerna</a>, as his successor. </p> 
      <h3><span id="Vicente_Cerna_y_Cerna_regime_.281865.E2.80.931871.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Vicente_Cerna_y_Cerna_regime_(1865–1871)">Vicente Cerna y Cerna regime (1865–1871)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Vicente Cerna y Cerna regime (1865–1871)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:VicenteCerna.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/VicenteCerna.jpg/170px-VicenteCerna.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="216" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1d/VicenteCerna.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="198" data-file-height="251"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:VicenteCerna.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Vicente_Cerna_y_Cerna" title="Vicente Cerna y Cerna">Vicente Cerna y Cerna</a> was the president of Guatemala from 1865 to 1871.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Vicente_Cerna_y_Cerna" title="Vicente Cerna y Cerna">Vicente Cerna y Cerna</a>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Vicente_Cerna_y_Cerna" title="Vicente Cerna y Cerna">Vicente Cerna y Cerna</a> was <a href="/wiki/President_of_Guatemala" title="President of Guatemala">president of Guatemala</a> from 24 May 1865 to 29 June 1871.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Liberal author <a href="/w/index.php?title=Alfonso_Enrique_Barrientos&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Alfonso Enrique Barrientos (page does not exist)">Alfonso Enrique Barrientos</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&nbsp;[<a href="https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alfonso_Enrique_Barrientos" class="extiw" title="es:Alfonso Enrique Barrientos">es</a>]</span>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrientos1948106_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrientos1948106-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed (July 2015)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> described Marshall Cerna's government in the following manner:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrientoss.f.106_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrientoss.f.106-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed (July 2015)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>A conservative and archaic government, badly organized and with worse intentions, was in charge of the country, centralizing all powers in Vicente Cerna, ambitious military man, who not happy with the general rank, had promoted himself to the Army Marshall rank, even though that rank did not exist and it does not exist in the Guatemalan military. The Marshall called himself President of the Republic, but in reality he was the foreman of oppressed and savaged people, cowardly enough that they had not dared to tell the dictator to leave threatening him with a revolution.</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>The State and Church were a single unit, and the conservative régime was strongly allied to the power of <a href="/wiki/Regular_clergy" title="Regular clergy">regular clergy</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>, who were then among the largest landowners in Guatemala. The tight relationship between church and state had been ratified by the <a href="/wiki/Concordat_of_1854" title="Concordat of 1854">Concordat of 1852</a>, which was the law until Cerna was deposed in 1871.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAycinena18542–16_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAycinena18542–16-70">[70]</a></sup> Even liberal generals like <a href="/w/index.php?title=Serapio_Cruz&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Serapio Cruz (page does not exist)">Serapio Cruz</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&nbsp;[<a href="https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Serapio_Cruz" class="extiw" title="es:Serapio Cruz">es</a>]</span> realized that Rafael Carrera's political and military presence made him practically invincible. Thus the generals fought under his command,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHernández_de_León1930_55-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHernández_de_León1930-55">[55]</a></sup> and waited—for a long time—until Carrera's death before beginning their revolt against the tamer Cerna.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008428_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGonzález_Davison2008428-71">[71]</a></sup> During Cerna's presidency, liberal party members were prosecuted and sent into exile; among them, those who started the Liberal Revolution of 1871.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHernández_de_León1930_55-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHernández_de_León1930-55">[55]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1871, the merchants guild, Consulado de Comercio, lost their exclusive court privilege. They had major effects on the economics of the time, and therefore land management. From 1839 to 1871, the Consulado held a consistent monopolistic position in the regime.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Liberal_governments_.281871.E2.80.931898.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Liberal_governments_(1871–1898)">Liberal governments (1871–1898)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Liberal governments (1871–1898)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Justo_Rufino_Barrios" title="Justo Rufino Barrios">Justo Rufino Barrios</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Guatemala's "Liberal Revolution" came in 1871 under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Justo_Rufino_Barrios" title="Justo Rufino Barrios">Justo Rufino Barrios</a>, who worked to modernize the country, improve trade, and introduce new crops and manufacturing. During this era coffee became an important crop for Guatemala.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoster2000173–175_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoster2000173–175-73">[73]</a></sup> Barrios had ambitions of reuniting Central America and took the country to war in an unsuccessful attempt to attain it, losing his life on the battlefield in 1885 against forces in El Salvador. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Manuel_Barillas" title="Manuel Barillas">Manuel Barillas</a> was president from 16 March 1886 to 15 March 1892. Manuel Barillas was unique among liberal presidents of Guatemala between 1871 and 1944: he handed over power to his successor peacefully. When election time approached, he sent for the three Liberal candidates to ask them what their government plan would be.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_los_Ríos194878_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_los_Ríos194878-74">[74]</a></sup> Happy with what he heard from general <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Mar%C3%ADa_Reyna_Barrios" class="mw-redirect" title="José María Reyna Barrios">Reyna Barrios</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_los_Ríos194878_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_los_Ríos194878-74">[74]</a></sup> Barillas made sure that a huge column of Quetzaltenango and Totonicapán indigenous people came down from the mountains to vote for him. Reyna was elected president. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_los_Ríos194882_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_los_Ríos194882-75">[75]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Mar%C3%ADa_Reina_Barrios" title="José María Reina Barrios">José María Reina Barrios</a> was President between 1892 and 1898. During Barrios's first term in office, the power of the landowners over the rural peasantry increased. He oversaw the rebuilding of parts of <a href="/wiki/Guatemala_City" title="Guatemala City">Guatemala City</a> on a grander scale, with wide, Parisian-style avenues. He oversaw Guatemala hosting the first "<a href="/wiki/Exposici%C3%B3n_Centroamericana" title="Exposición Centroamericana">Exposición Centroamericana</a>" ("Central American Fair") in 1897. During his second term, Barrios printed <a href="/wiki/Bond_(finance)" title="Bond (finance)">bonds</a> to fund his ambitious plans, fueling <a href="/wiki/Monetary_inflation" title="Monetary inflation">monetary inflation</a> and the rise of popular opposition to his regime. </p>
      <p>His administration also worked on improving the roads, installing national and international telegraphs and introducing electricity to Guatemala City. Completing a transoceanic railway was a main objective of his government, with a goal to attract international investors at a time when the <a href="/wiki/Panama_Canal" title="Panama Canal">Panama Canal</a> was not built yet. </p> 
      <h3><span id="Manuel_Estrada_Cabrera_regime_.281898.E2.80.931920.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Manuel_Estrada_Cabrera_regime_(1898–1920)">Manuel Estrada Cabrera regime (1898–1920)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Manuel Estrada Cabrera regime (1898–1920)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Estrada_Cabrera" title="Manuel Estrada Cabrera">Manuel Estrada Cabrera</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Manuel_Estrada_Cabrera.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Manuel_Estrada_Cabrera.jpg/170px-Manuel_Estrada_Cabrera.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Manuel_Estrada_Cabrera.jpg/255px-Manuel_Estrada_Cabrera.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Manuel_Estrada_Cabrera.jpg/340px-Manuel_Estrada_Cabrera.jpg 2x" data-file-width="587" data-file-height="799"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Manuel_Estrada_Cabrera.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Manuel_Estrada_Cabrera" title="Manuel Estrada Cabrera">Manuel Estrada Cabrera</a> ruled Guatemala between 1898 and 1920.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>After the assassination of general <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Mar%C3%ADa_Reina_Barrios" title="José María Reina Barrios">José María Reina Barrios</a> on 8 February 1898, the Guatemalan cabinet called an emergency meeting to appoint a new successor, but declined to invite Estrada Cabrera to the meeting, even though he was the designated successor to the Presidency. There are two different descriptions of how Cabrera was able to become president. The first states that Cabrera entered the cabinet meeting "with pistol drawn" to assert his entitlement to the presidency,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChapman200754_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChapman200754-76">[76]</a></sup> while the second states that he showed up unarmed to the meeting and demanded the presidency by virtue of being the designated successor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArévalo_Martinez194542_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArévalo_Martinez194542-77">[77]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The first civilian Guatemalan head of state in over 50 years, Estrada Cabrera overcame resistance to his regime by August 1898 and called for elections in September, which he won handily.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArévalo_Martinez194546_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArévalo_Martinez194546-78">[78]</a></sup> In 1898 the Legislature convened for the election of President Estrada Cabrera, who triumphed thanks to the large number of soldiers and policemen who went to vote in civilian clothes and to the large number of illiterate family that they brought with them to the polls.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETorres_Espinoza200742_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETorres_Espinoza200742-79">[79]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>One of Estrada Cabrera's most famous and most bitter legacies was allowing the entry of the <a href="/wiki/United_Fruit_Company" title="United Fruit Company">United Fruit Company</a> (UFCO) into the Guatemalan economic and political arena. As a member of the <a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_Latin_America" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberalism in Latin America">Liberal Party</a>, he sought to encourage development of the nation's infrastructure of <a href="/wiki/Highway" title="Highway">highways</a>, <a href="/wiki/Railroad" class="mw-redirect" title="Railroad">railroads</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sea_port" class="mw-redirect" title="Sea port">sea ports</a> for the sake of expanding the export economy. By the time Estrada Cabrera assumed the presidency there had been repeated efforts to construct a railroad from the major port of <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Barrios" title="Puerto Barrios">Puerto Barrios</a> to the capital, Guatemala City. Due to lack of funding exacerbated by the collapse of the internal coffee trade, the railway fell 100 kilometres (60&nbsp;mi) short of its goal. Estrada Cabrera decided, without consulting the legislature or judiciary, that striking a deal with the UFCO was the only way to finish the railway.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDosal1993_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDosal1993-80">[80]</a></sup> Cabrera signed a contract with UFCO's <a href="/wiki/Minor_Cooper_Keith" title="Minor Cooper Keith">Minor Cooper Keith</a> in 1904 that gave the company tax exemptions, land grants, and control of all railroads on the Atlantic side.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChapman2007_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChapman2007-81">[81]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Estrada Cabrera often employed brutal methods to assert his authority. Right at the beginning of his first presidential period he started prosecuting his political rivals and soon established a well-organized web of spies. One American ambassador returned to the United States after he learned the dictator had given orders to poison him. Former President <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Barillas" title="Manuel Barillas">Manuel Barillas</a> was stabbed to death in Mexico City. Estrada Cabrera responded violently to workers' strikes against UFCO. In one incident, when UFCO went directly to Estrada Cabrera to resolve a strike (after the armed forces refused to respond), the president ordered an armed unit to enter a workers' compound. The forces "arrived in the night, firing indiscriminately into the workers' sleeping quarters, wounding and killing an unspecified number."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChapman200783_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChapman200783-82">[82]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1906 Estrada faced serious revolts against his rule; the rebels were supported by the governments of some of the other <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central American</a> nations, but Estrada succeeded in putting them down. Elections were held by the people against the will of Estrada Cabrera and thus he had the president-elect murdered in retaliation. In 1907 Estrada narrowly survived an assassination attempt when a bomb exploded near his carriage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Aerenlund2006_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Aerenlund2006-83">[83]</a></sup> It has been suggested that the extreme despotic characteristics of Estrada did not emerge until after an attempt on his life in 1907.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArévalo_Martinez1945146_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArévalo_Martinez1945146-84">[84]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Guatemala_City" title="Guatemala City">Guatemala City</a> was badly damaged in the <a href="/wiki/1917_Guatemala_earthquake" title="1917 Guatemala earthquake">1917 Guatemala earthquake</a>. </p>
      <p>Estrada Cabrera continued in power until forced to resign after new revolts in 1920. By that time his power had declined drastically and he was reliant upon the loyalty of a few generals. While the United States threatened intervention if he was removed through revolution, a bipartisan coalition came together to remove him from the presidency. He was removed from office after the national assembly charged that he was mentally incompetent, and appointed Carlos Herrera in his place on 8 April 1920.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDosal199327_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDosal199327-85">[85]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Jorge_Ubico_regime_.281931.E2.80.931944.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Jorge_Ubico_regime_(1931–1944)">Jorge Ubico regime (1931–1944)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Jorge Ubico regime (1931–1944)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jorge_Ubico" title="Jorge Ubico">Jorge Ubico</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> began in 1929 and badly damaged the Guatemalan economy, causing a rise in <a href="/wiki/Unemployment" title="Unemployment">unemployment</a>, and leading to unrest among workers and laborers. Afraid of a popular revolt, the Guatemalan landed elite lent their support to <a href="/wiki/Jorge_Ubico" title="Jorge Ubico">Jorge Ubico</a>, who had become well known for "efficiency and cruelty" as a provincial governor. Ubico won the election that followed in 1931, in which he was the only candidate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForster200112–15_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForster200112–15-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGleijeses199110–11_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGleijeses199110–11-87">[87]</a></sup> After his election his policies quickly became authoritarian. He replaced the system of debt <a href="/wiki/Peon" title="Peon">peonage</a> with a brutally enforced <a href="/wiki/Vagrancy_(people)" class="mw-redirect" title="Vagrancy (people)">vagrancy</a> law, requiring all men of working age who did not own land to work a minimum of 100 days of hard labor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForster200129_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForster200129-88">[88]</a></sup> His government used unpaid Indian labor to build roads and railways. Ubico also froze wages at very low levels, and passed a law allowing land-owners complete immunity from prosecution for any action they took to defend their property,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForster200129_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForster200129-88">[88]</a></sup> an action described by historians as legalizing murder.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGleijeses199113_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGleijeses199113-89">[89]</a></sup> He greatly strengthened the police force, turning it into one of the most efficient and ruthless in Latin America.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGleijeses199117_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGleijeses199117-90">[90]</a></sup> He gave them greater authority to shoot and imprison people suspected of breaking the labor laws. These laws created tremendous resentment against him among agricultural laborers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForster200129–32_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForster200129–32-91">[91]</a></sup> The government became highly militarized; under his rule, every provincial governor was a general in the army.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGleijeses199114_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGleijeses199114-92">[92]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Ubico continued his predecessor's policy of making massive concessions to the <a href="/wiki/United_Fruit_Company" title="United Fruit Company">United Fruit Company</a>, often at a cost to Guatemala. He granted the company 200,000 hectares (490,000 acres) of public land in exchange for a promise to build a port, a promise he later waived.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGleijeses199122_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGleijeses199122-93">[93]</a></sup> Since its entry into Guatemala, the United Fruit Company had expanded its land-holdings by displacing farmers and converting their farmland to <a href="/wiki/Banana_plantation" title="Banana plantation">banana plantations</a>. This process accelerated under Ubico's presidency, with the government doing nothing to stop it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForster200119_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForster200119-94">[94]</a></sup> The company received import duty and real estate tax exemptions from the government and controlled more land than any other individual or group. It also controlled the sole railroad in the country, the sole facilities capable of producing electricity, and the port facilities at <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Barrios" title="Puerto Barrios">Puerto Barrios</a> on the Atlantic coast.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesingerKinzer199967–71_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesingerKinzer199967–71-95">[95]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Ubico saw the United States as an ally against the supposed communist threat of Mexico, and made efforts to gain its support.
