When the US [United States declaration of war upon Germany (1941)|declared war]] against Germany in 1941, Ubico acted on American instructions and arrested all people in Guatemala of <a href="/wiki/German_Guatemalan" title="German Guatemalan">German descent</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGleijeses199120_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGleijeses199120-96">[96]</a></sup> He also permitted the US to establish an air base in Guatemala, with the stated aim of protecting the <a href="/wiki/Panama_Canal" title="Panama Canal">Panama Canal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEImmerman198237_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEImmerman198237-97">[97]</a></sup> However, Ubico was an admirer of European <a href="/wiki/Fascism" title="Fascism">fascists</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Franco" title="Francisco Franco">Francisco Franco</a> and <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGleijeses199119_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGleijeses199119-98">[98]</a></sup> and considered himself to be "another <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_Bonaparte" class="mw-redirect" title="Napoleon Bonaparte">Napoleon</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_los_Ríos194898_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_los_Ríos194898-99">[99]</a></sup> He occasionally compared himself to <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShillington200238–39_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShillington200238–39-100">[100]</a></sup> He dressed ostentatiously and surrounded himself with statues and paintings of Napoleon, regularly commenting on the similarities between their appearances. He militarized numerous political and social institutions—including the post office, schools, and symphony orchestras—and placed military officers in charge of many government posts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStreeter200011–12_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStreeter200011–12-101">[101]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEImmerman198232_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEImmerman198232-102">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrandin2000195_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrandin2000195-103">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenz199616–17_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenz199616–17-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELovemanDavies1997118–120_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELovemanDavies1997118–120-105">[105]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Guatemalan_Revolution_.281944.E2.80.931954.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Guatemalan_Revolution_(1944–1954)">Guatemalan Revolution (1944–1954)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Guatemalan Revolution (1944–1954)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Guatemalan_Revolution" title="Guatemalan Revolution">Guatemalan Revolution</a>
      </div> 
      <p>On 1 July 1944 Ubico was forced to resign from the presidency in response to a wave of protests and a <a href="/wiki/General_strike" title="General strike">general strike</a> inspired by brutal labor conditions among plantation workers.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> His chosen replacement, General <a href="/wiki/Juan_Federico_Ponce_Vaides" title="Juan Federico Ponce Vaides">Juan Federico Ponce Vaides</a>, was forced out of office on 20 October 1944 by a <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d'état">coup d'état</a> led by Major <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Javier_Arana" title="Francisco Javier Arana">Francisco Javier Arana</a> and Captain <a href="/wiki/Jacobo_%C3%81rbenz_Guzm%C3%A1n" class="mw-redirect" title="Jacobo Árbenz Guzmán">Jacobo Árbenz Guzmán</a>. About 100 people were killed in the coup. The country was then led by a <a href="/wiki/Military_junta" title="Military junta">military junta</a> made up of Arana, Árbenz, and <a href="/wiki/Jorge_Toriello_Garrido" title="Jorge Toriello Garrido">Jorge Toriello Garrido</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForster200189–91_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForster200189–91-107">[107]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Jacoboarbenz1950.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Jacoboarbenz1950.jpg/220px-Jacoboarbenz1950.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Jacoboarbenz1950.jpg/330px-Jacoboarbenz1950.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Jacoboarbenz1950.jpg/440px-Jacoboarbenz1950.jpg 2x" data-file-width="892" data-file-height="892"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Jacoboarbenz1950.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Guatemala's democratically elected president <a href="/wiki/Jacobo_%C3%81rbenz" title="Jacobo Árbenz">Jacobo Árbenz</a> was overthrown in a coup planned by the <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">CIA</a> to protect the profits of the <a href="/wiki/United_Fruit_Company" title="United Fruit Company">United Fruit Company</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The junta organized Guatemala's first free election, which the philosophically conservative writer and teacher <a href="/wiki/Juan_Jos%C3%A9_Ar%C3%A9valo" title="Juan José Arévalo">Juan José Arévalo</a>, who wanted to turn the country into a <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberal</a> <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalist</a> society won with a majority of 86%.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStreeter200012–15_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStreeter200012–15-108">[108]</a></sup> His "<a href="/wiki/Christian_socialism" title="Christian socialism">Christian Socialist</a>" policies were inspired to a large extent by the U.S. <a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">New Deal</a> of President <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Chomsky_1985_154–160_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chomsky_1985_154–160-109">[109]</a></sup> Arévalo built new health centers, increased funding for education, and drafted a more liberal labor law,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStreeter200014–15_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStreeter200014–15-110">[110]</a></sup> while criminalizing unions in workplaces with less than 500 workers,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForster200198–99_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForster200198–99-111">[111]</a></sup> and cracking down on communists.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStreeter200015–16_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStreeter200015–16-112">[112]</a></sup> Although Arévalo was popular among nationalists, he had enemies in the church and the military, and faced at least 25 coup attempts during his presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStreeter200016–17_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStreeter200016–17-113">[113]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Arévalo was constitutionally prohibited from contesting the 1950 elections. The largely free and fair elections were won by <a href="/wiki/Jacobo_%C3%81rbenz_Guzm%C3%A1n" class="mw-redirect" title="Jacobo Árbenz Guzmán">Jacobo Árbenz Guzmán</a>, Arévalo's defense minister.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGleijeses199173–84_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGleijeses199173–84-114">[114]</a></sup> Árbenz continued the moderate capitalist approach of Arévalo.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStreeter200018–19_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStreeter200018–19-115">[115]</a></sup> His most important policy was <a href="/wiki/Decree_900" title="Decree 900">Decree 900</a>, a sweeping agrarian reform bill passed in 1952.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEImmerman198264–67_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEImmerman198264–67-116">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGleijeses1991144–146_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGleijeses1991144–146-117">[117]</a></sup> Decree 900 transferred uncultivated land to landless peasants.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEImmerman198264–67_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEImmerman198264–67-116">[116]</a></sup> Only 1,710 of the nearly 350,000 private land-holdings were affected by the law,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGleijeses1991149–164_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGleijeses1991149–164-118">[118]</a></sup> which benefited approximately 500,000 individuals, or one-sixth of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGleijeses1991149–164_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGleijeses1991149–164-118">[118]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Coup_and_civil_war_.281954.E2.80.931996.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Coup_and_civil_war_(1954–1996)">Coup and civil war (1954–1996)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Coup and civil war (1954–1996)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/1954_Guatemalan_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1954 Guatemalan coup d'état">1954 Guatemalan coup d'état</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guatemalan_Civil_War" title="Guatemalan Civil War">Guatemalan Civil War</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/United_States_involvement_in_regime_change" title="United States involvement in regime change">United States involvement in regime change</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Despite their popularity within the country, the reforms of the Guatemalan Revolution were disliked by the United States government, which was predisposed by the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> to see it as communist, and the <a href="/wiki/United_Fruit_Company" title="United Fruit Company">United Fruit Company</a> (UFCO), whose hugely profitable business had been affected by the end to brutal labor practices.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStreeter200015–16_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStreeter200015–16-112">[112]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEImmerman198248–50_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEImmerman198248–50-119">[119]</a></sup> The attitude of the U.S. government was also influenced by a propaganda campaign carried out by the UFCO.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaterson2009304_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaterson2009304-120">[120]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Harry_Truman" class="mw-redirect" title="Harry Truman">Harry Truman</a> authorized <a href="/wiki/Operation_PBFortune" title="Operation PBFortune">Operation PBFortune</a> to topple Árbenz in 1952, with the support of Nicaraguan dictator <a href="/wiki/Anastasio_Somoza_Garc%C3%ADa" title="Anastasio Somoza García">Anastasio Somoza García</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesingerKinzer1999102_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesingerKinzer1999102-121">[121]</a></sup> but the operation was aborted when too many details became public.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesingerKinzer1999102_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesingerKinzer1999102-121">[121]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGleijeses1991228–231_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGleijeses1991228–231-122">[122]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a> was elected U.S. President in 1952, promising to take a harder line against communism; the close links that his staff members <a href="/wiki/John_Foster_Dulles" title="John Foster Dulles">John Foster Dulles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Allen_Dulles" title="Allen Dulles">Allen Dulles</a> had to the UFCO also predisposed him to act against Árbenz.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEImmerman1982122–127_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEImmerman1982122–127-123">[123]</a></sup> Eisenhower authorized the CIA to carry out Operation PBSuccess in August 1953. The CIA armed, funded, and trained a force of 480 men led by <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Castillo_Armas" title="Carlos Castillo Armas">Carlos Castillo Armas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEImmerman1982161–170_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEImmerman1982161–170-124">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesingerKinzer1999171–175_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesingerKinzer1999171–175-125">[125]</a></sup> The force invaded Guatemala on 18 June 1954, backed by a heavy campaign of <a href="/wiki/Psychological_warfare" title="Psychological warfare">psychological warfare</a>, including bombings of <a href="/wiki/Guatemala_City" title="Guatemala City">Guatemala City</a> and an anti-Árbenz radio station claiming to be genuine news.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEImmerman1982161–170_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEImmerman1982161–170-124">[124]</a></sup> The invasion force fared poorly militarily, but the psychological warfare and the possibility of a U.S. invasion intimidated the Guatemalan army, which refused to fight. Árbenz resigned on 27 June.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEImmerman1982173–178_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEImmerman1982173–178-126">[126]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchlesingerKinzer1999190–204_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesingerKinzer1999190–204-127">[127]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Following negotiations in <a href="/wiki/San_Salvador" title="San Salvador">San Salvador</a>, Carlos Castillo Armas became President on 7 July 1954.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEImmerman1982173–178_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEImmerman1982173–178-126">[126]</a></sup> Elections were held in early October, from which all political parties were barred from participating. Castillo Armas was the only candidate and won the election with 99% of the vote.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEImmerman1982173–178_126-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEImmerman1982173–178-126">[126]</a></sup> Castillo Armas reversed <a href="/wiki/Decree_900" title="Decree 900">Decree 900</a> and ruled until 26 July 1957, when he was assassinated by Romeo Vásquez, a member of his personal guard. After the rigged<sup id="cite_ref-Chomsky_1985_154–160_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chomsky_1985_154–160-109">[109]</a></sup> election that followed, General <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Miguel_Ram%C3%B3n_Yd%C3%ADgoras_Fuentes" class="mw-redirect" title="José Miguel Ramón Ydígoras Fuentes">Miguel Ydígoras Fuentes</a> assumed power. He is celebrated for challenging the Mexican president to a gentleman's <a href="/wiki/Duel" title="Duel">duel</a> on the bridge on the south border to end a feud on the subject of illegal fishing by Mexican boats on Guatemala's Pacific coast, two of which were sunk by the Guatemalan Air Force. Ydigoras authorized the training of 5,000 anti-<a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">Castro</a> <a href="/wiki/Cubans" title="Cubans">Cubans</a> in Guatemala. He also provided airstrips in the region of <a href="/wiki/Pet%C3%A9n_(department)" class="mw-redirect" title="Petén (department)">Petén</a> for what later became the US-sponsored, failed <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion" title="Bay of Pigs Invasion">Bay of Pigs Invasion</a> in 1961. Ydigoras' government was ousted in 1963 when the Guatemalan Air Force attacked several military bases; the coup was led by his Defense Minister, Colonel <a href="/wiki/Enrique_Peralta_Azurdia" title="Enrique Peralta Azurdia">Enrique Peralta Azurdia</a>. </p>
      <p>In 1963, the junta called an election, which permitted Arevalo to return from exile and run. However, a coup from within the military, backed by the <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Administration" class="mw-redirect" title="Kennedy Administration">Kennedy Administration</a>, prevented the election from taking place, and forestalled a likely victory for Arevalo. The new régime intensified the campaign of terror against the guerrillas that had begun under Ydígoras-Fuentes.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1966, <a href="/wiki/Julio_C%C3%A9sar_M%C3%A9ndez_Montenegro" title="Julio César Méndez Montenegro">Julio César Méndez Montenegro</a> was elected president of Guatemala under the banner "Democratic Opening". Mendez Montenegro was the candidate of the Revolutionary Party, a center-left party that had its origins in the post-Ubico era. During this time rightist <a href="/wiki/Paramilitary" title="Paramilitary">paramilitary</a> organizations, such as the "White Hand" (<i><a href="/wiki/Mano_Blanca" title="Mano Blanca">Mano Blanca</a></i>), and the Anticommunist Secret Army (<i>Ejército Secreto Anticomunista</i>) were formed. Those groups were the forerunners of the infamous "<a href="/wiki/Death_squad" title="Death squad">Death Squads</a>". Military advisers from the <a href="/wiki/Special_Forces_(United_States_Army)" class="mw-redirect" title="Special Forces (United States Army)">United States Army Special Forces</a> (Green Berets) were sent to Guatemala to train these troops and help transform the army into a modern counter-insurgency force, which eventually made it the most sophisticated in Central America.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1970, Colonel <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Manuel_Arana_Osorio" title="Carlos Manuel Arana Osorio">Carlos Manuel Arana Osorio</a> was elected president. By 1972, members of the guerrilla movement entered the country from Mexico and settled in the Western Highlands. In the <a href="/wiki/Guatemalan_general_election,_1974" class="mw-redirect" title="Guatemalan general election, 1974">disputed election of 1974</a>, General <a href="/wiki/Kjell_Eugenio_Laugerud_Garc%C3%ADa" title="Kjell Eugenio Laugerud García">Kjell Laugerud García</a> defeated General <a href="/wiki/Efra%C3%ADn_R%C3%ADos_Montt" title="Efraín Ríos Montt">Efraín Ríos Montt</a>, a candidate of the Christian Democratic Party, who claimed that he had been cheated out of a victory through fraud. </p>
      <p>On 4 February 1976, a major earthquake destroyed several cities and caused more than 25,000 deaths, especially among the poor, whose housing was substandard. The government's failure to respond rapidly to the aftermath of the earthquake and to relieve homelessness gave rise to widespread discontent, which contributed to growing popular unrest. General <a href="/wiki/Romeo_Lucas_Garc%C3%ADa" class="mw-redirect" title="Romeo Lucas García">Romeo Lucas García</a> assumed power in 1978 in a fraudulent election. </p>
      <p>The 1970s saw the rise of two new guerrilla organizations, the Guerrilla Army of the Poor (EGP) and the Organization of the People in Arms (ORPA). They began guerrilla attacks that included urban and rural warfare, mainly against the military and some civilian supporters of the army. The army and the paramilitary forces responded with a brutal counter-insurgency campaign that resulted in tens of thousands of civilian deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaFeber1993165_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaFeber1993165-130">[130]</a></sup> In 1979, the U.S. president, <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a>, who had until then been providing public support for the government forces, ordered a ban on all military aid to the Guatemalan Army because of its widespread and systematic abuse of human rights.<sup id="cite_ref-Chomsky_1985_154–160_109-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chomsky_1985_154–160-109">[109]</a></sup> However, documents have since come to light that suggest that American aid continued throughout the Carter years, through clandestine channels.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Memorial_Rio_Negro.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Memorial_Rio_Negro.jpg/220px-Memorial_Rio_Negro.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Memorial_Rio_Negro.jpg/330px-Memorial_Rio_Negro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Memorial_Rio_Negro.jpg/440px-Memorial_Rio_Negro.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="450"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Memorial_Rio_Negro.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Memorial to the victims of the <a href="/wiki/R%C3%ADo_Negro_massacres" title="Río Negro massacres">Río Negro massacres</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>On 31 January 1980, a group of indigenous <a href="/wiki/K%27iche%27_people" class="mw-redirect" title="K'iche' people">K'iche'</a> took over the Spanish Embassy to protest army massacres in the countryside. The Guatemalan government armed forces launched an assault that killed almost everyone inside in a <a href="/wiki/Burning_of_the_Spanish_Embassy" class="mw-redirect" title="Burning of the Spanish Embassy">fire that consumed the building</a>. The Guatemalan government claimed that the activists set the fire, thus <a href="/wiki/Self-immolation" title="Self-immolation">immolating</a> themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> However the Spanish ambassador survived the fire and disputed this claim, saying that the Guatemalan police intentionally killed almost everyone inside and set the fire to erase traces of their acts. As a result, the government of Spain broke off diplomatic relations with Guatemala. </p>
      <p>This government was overthrown in 1982 and General <a href="/wiki/Efra%C3%ADn_R%C3%ADos_Montt" title="Efraín Ríos Montt">Efraín Ríos Montt</a> was named President of the military junta. He continued the bloody campaign of torture, <a href="/wiki/Forced_disappearance" title="Forced disappearance">forced disappearances</a>, and "<a href="/wiki/Scorched_earth" title="Scorched earth">scorched earth</a>" warfare. The country became a <a href="/wiki/Pariah_state" title="Pariah state">pariah state</a> internationally, although the regime received considerable support from the <a href="/wiki/Reagan_Administration" class="mw-redirect" title="Reagan Administration">Reagan Administration</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> and Reagan himself described Ríos Montt as "a man of great personal integrity."<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Ríos Montt was overthrown by General <a href="/wiki/%C3%93scar_Humberto_Mej%C3%ADa_Victores" class="mw-redirect" title="Óscar Humberto Mejía Victores">Óscar Humberto Mejía Victores</a>, who called for an election of a national constituent assembly to write a new constitution, leading to a free election in 1986, won by <a href="/wiki/Marco_Vinicio_Cerezo_Ar%C3%A9valo" class="mw-redirect" title="Marco Vinicio Cerezo Arévalo">Vinicio Cerezo Arévalo</a>, the candidate of the Christian Democracy Party. </p>
      <p>In 1982, the four guerrilla groups, EGP, ORPA, FAR and PGT, merged and formed the <a href="/wiki/Guatemalan_National_Revolutionary_Unity" title="Guatemalan National Revolutionary Unity">URNG</a>, influenced by the <a href="/wiki/El_Salvador" title="El Salvador">Salvadoran</a> guerrilla <a href="/wiki/Farabundo_Mart%C3%AD_National_Liberation_Front" title="Farabundo Martí National Liberation Front">FMLN</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">Nicaraguan</a> <a href="/wiki/Sandinista_National_Liberation_Front" title="Sandinista National Liberation Front">FSLN</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>'s government, in order to become stronger. As a result of the Army's "scorched earth" tactics in the countryside, more than 45,000 Guatemalans fled across the border to Mexico. The Mexican government placed the refugees in camps in <a href="/wiki/Chiapas" title="Chiapas">Chiapas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tabasco" title="Tabasco">Tabasco</a>. </p>
      <p>In 1992, the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a> was awarded to <a href="/wiki/Rigoberta_Mench%C3%BA" title="Rigoberta Menchú">Rigoberta Menchú</a> for her efforts to bring international attention to the government-sponsored <a href="/wiki/Guatemalan_genocide" title="Guatemalan genocide">genocide against the indigenous population</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="1996.E2.80.932000"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1996–2000">1996–2000</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: 1996–2000">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Chichicastenango_market_2009.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Chichicastenango_market_2009.JPG/220px-Chichicastenango_market_2009.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Chichicastenango_market_2009.JPG/330px-Chichicastenango_market_2009.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Chichicastenango_market_2009.JPG/440px-Chichicastenango_market_2009.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4288" data-file-height="2848"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Chichicastenango_market_2009.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>An outdoor market in <a href="/wiki/Chichicastenango" title="Chichicastenango">Chichicastenango</a>, 2009.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Guatemalan_Civil_War" title="Guatemalan Civil War">Guatemalan Civil War</a> ended in 1996 with a peace accord between the guerrillas and the government, negotiated by the United Nations through intense brokerage by nations such as Norway and Spain. Both sides made major concessions. The guerrilla fighters disarmed and received land to work. According to the U.N.-sponsored <a href="/wiki/Truth_commission" title="Truth commission">truth commission</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Commission_for_Historical_Clarification" class="mw-redirect" title="Commission for Historical Clarification">Commission for Historical Clarification</a>), government forces and state-sponsored, CIA-trained paramilitaries were responsible for over 93% of the human rights violations during the war.<sup id="cite_ref-RightsViolations_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RightsViolations-136">[136]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the last few years, millions of documents related to crimes committed during the civil war have been found abandoned by the former Guatemalan police. The families of over 45,000 Guatemalan activists who <a href="/wiki/Forced_Disappearance" class="mw-redirect" title="Forced Disappearance">disappeared</a> during the civil war are now reviewing the documents, which have been digitized. This could lead to further legal actions.<sup id="cite_ref-PoliceDocuments_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PoliceDocuments-137">[137]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During the first ten years of the civil war, the victims of the state-sponsored terror were primarily students, workers, professionals, and opposition figures, but in the last years they were thousands of mostly rural <a href="/wiki/Maya_peoples" title="Maya peoples">Maya</a> farmers and non-combatants. More than 450 Maya villages were destroyed and over 1 million people became refugees or displaced within Guatemala. </p>
      <p>In 1995, the Catholic Archdiocese of Guatemala began the Recovery of Historical Memory (REMHI) project,<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> known in Spanish as "El Proyecto de la Recuperación de la Memoria Histórica", to collect the facts and history of Guatemala's long civil war and confront the truth of those years. On 24 April 1998, REMHI presented the results of its work in the report "Guatemala: Nunca Más!". This report summarized testimony and statements of thousands of witnesses and victims of repression during the Civil War. "The report laid the blame for 80 per cent of the atrocities at the door of the Guatemalan Army and its collaborators within the social and political elite."<sup id="cite_ref-Stanford,_Peter_2008_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stanford,_Peter_2008-139">[139]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Catholic Bishop Juan José Gerardi Conedera worked on the Recovery of Historical Memory Project and two days after he announced the release of its report on victims of the Guatemalan Civil War, "Guatemala: Nunca Más!", in April 1998, Bishop Gerardi was attacked in his garage and beaten to death.<sup id="cite_ref-Stanford,_Peter_2008_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stanford,_Peter_2008-139">[139]</a></sup> In 2001, in the first trial in a civilian court of members of the military in Guatemalan history, three Army officers were convicted of his death and sentenced to 30 years in prison. A priest was convicted as an accomplice and was sentenced to 20 years in prison.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>According to the report, <i>Recuperación de la Memoria Histórica</i> (REMHI), some 200,000 people died. More than one million people were forced to flee their homes and hundreds of villages were destroyed. The Historical Clarification Commission attributed more than 93% of all documented violations of human rights to Guatemala's military government, and estimated that Maya Indians accounted for 83% of the victims. It concluded in 1999 that state actions constituted genocide.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In some areas such as <a href="/wiki/Baja_Verapaz" class="mw-redirect" title="Baja Verapaz">Baja Verapaz</a>, the Truth Commission found that the Guatemalan state engaged in an intentional policy of genocide against particular ethnic groups in the <a href="/wiki/Guatemalan_Civil_War" title="Guatemalan Civil War">Civil War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RightsViolations_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RightsViolations-136">[136]</a></sup> In 1999, U.S. president <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a> said that the United States had been wrong to have provided support to the Guatemalan military forces that took part in these brutal civilian killings.<sup id="cite_ref-Babington1999_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Babington1999-143">[143]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Since_2000">Since 2000</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Since 2000">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Since the peace accords Guatemala has had both economic growth and successive democratic elections, most recently in 2019. In the 2019 elections, <a href="/wiki/Alejandro_Giammattei" title="Alejandro Giammattei">Alejandro Giammattei</a> won the presidency. He assumed office in January 2020. </p>
      <p>In January 2012 Efrain Rios Montt, the former dictator of Guatemala, appeared in a Guatemalan court on genocide charges. During the hearing, the government presented evidence of over 100 incidents involving at least 1,771 deaths, 1,445 rapes, and the displacement of nearly 30,000 Guatemalans during his 17-month rule from 1982 to 1983. The prosecution wanted him incarcerated because he was viewed as a flight risk but he remained free on bail, under house arrest and guarded by the Guatemalan National Civil Police (PNC). On 10 May 2013, Rios Montt was found guilty and sentenced to 80 years in prison. It marked the first time that a national court had found a former head of state guilty of genocide.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> The conviction was later overturned, and Montt's trial resumed in January 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> In August 2015, a Guatemalan court ruled that Rios Montt could stand trial for genocide and crimes against humanity, but that he could not be sentenced due to his age and deteriorating health.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Ex-President <a href="/wiki/Alfonso_Portillo" title="Alfonso Portillo">Alfonso Portillo</a> was arrested in January 2010 while trying to flee Guatemala. He was acquitted in May 2010, by a panel of judges that threw out some of the evidence and discounted certain witnesses as unreliable.<sup id="cite_ref-icpor_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-icpor-147">[147]</a></sup> The Guatemalan Attorney-General, <a href="/wiki/Claudia_Paz_y_Paz" title="Claudia Paz y Paz">Claudia Paz y Paz</a>, called the verdict "a terrible message of injustice," and "a wake up call about the power structures." In its appeal, the <a href="/wiki/International_Commission_Against_Impunity_in_Guatemala" class="mw-redirect" title="International Commission Against Impunity in Guatemala">International Commission Against Impunity in Guatemala</a> (CICIG), a UN judicial group assisting the Guatemalan government, called the decision's assessment of the meticulously-documented evidence against Portillo Cabrera "whimsical" and said the decision's assertion that the president of Guatemala and his ministers had no responsibility for handling public funds ran counter to the constitution and laws of Guatemala.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> A New York grand jury had indicted Portillo Cabrera in 2009 for embezzlement; following his acquittal on those charges in Guatemala that country's Supreme Court authorized his extradition to the US.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> The Guatemalan judiciary is deeply corrupt and the selection committee for new nominations has been captured by criminal elements.<sup id="cite_ref-icpor_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-icpor-147">[147]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Zona_14_de_Guatemala_City.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Zona_14_de_Guatemala_City.jpg/220px-Zona_14_de_Guatemala_City.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="99" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Zona_14_de_Guatemala_City.jpg/330px-Zona_14_de_Guatemala_City.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Zona_14_de_Guatemala_City.jpg/440px-Zona_14_de_Guatemala_City.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="920"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Zona_14_de_Guatemala_City.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Guatemala_City" title="Guatemala City">Guatemala City</a> is the capital and largest city of Guatemala and the most populous urban area in <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <h3><span id="P.C3.A9rez_Molina_government_and_.22La_L.C3.ADnea.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Pérez_Molina_government_and_&quot;La_Línea&quot;">Pérez Molina government and "La Línea"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Pérez Molina government and &quot;La Línea&quot;">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Otto_P%C3%A9rez_Molina" title="Otto Pérez Molina">Otto Pérez Molina</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Retired general <a href="/wiki/Otto_P%C3%A9rez_Molina" title="Otto Pérez Molina">Otto Pérez Molina</a> was elected president in 2011 along with <a href="/wiki/Roxana_Baldetti" title="Roxana Baldetti">Roxana Baldetti</a>, the first woman ever elected vice-president in Guatemala; they began their term in office on 14 January 2012. But on 16 April 2015, a United Nations (UN) anti-corruption agency report implicated several high-profile politicians including Baldetti's private secretary, Juan Carlos Monzón, and the director of the Guatemalan Internal Revenue Service (SAT).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (July 2016)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup><sup id="cite_ref-veliz_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-veliz-151">[151]</a></sup> The revelations provoked more public outrage than had been seen since the presidency of General <a href="/wiki/Kjell_Eugenio_Laugerud_Garc%C3%ADa" title="Kjell Eugenio Laugerud García">Kjell Eugenio Laugerud García</a>. The <a href="/wiki/International_Commission_against_Impunity_in_Guatemala" title="International Commission against Impunity in Guatemala">International Commission against Impunity in Guatemala</a> (CICIG) worked with the Guatemalan attorney-general to reveal the scam known as "<a href="/wiki/La_L%C3%ADnea_corruption_case" title="La Línea corruption case">La Línea</a>", following a year-long investigation that included <a href="/wiki/Telephone_tapping" title="Telephone tapping">wire taps</a>. </p>
      <p>Officials received bribes from importers in exchange for discounted import tariffs,<sup id="cite_ref-veliz_151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-veliz-151">[151]</a></sup> a practice rooted in a long tradition of customs corruption in the country, as a fund-raising tactic of successive military governments for counterinsurgency operations during Guatemala's 36-year-long <a href="/wiki/Guatemala_Civil_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Guatemala Civil War">civil war</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-linea10junio_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-linea10junio-152">[152]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Solano22abril_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Solano22abril-153">[153]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>A Facebook event using the hashtag <i>#RenunciaYa</i> (Resign Now) invited citizens to go downtown in <a href="/wiki/Guatemala_City" title="Guatemala City">Guatemala City</a> to ask for Baldetti's resignation. Within days, over 10,000 people RSVPed that they would attend. Organisers made clear that no political party or group was behind the event, and instructed protesters at the event to follow the law. They also urged people to bring water, food and sunblock, but not to cover their faces or wear political party colors.<sup id="cite_ref-ollantay21mayo_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ollantay21mayo-154">[154]</a></sup> Tens of thousands of people took to the streets of Guatemala City. They protested in front of the presidential palace. Baldetti resigned a few days later. She was forced to remain in Guatemala when the United States revoked her visa. The Guatemalan government arraigned her, since it had enough evidence to suspect her involvement in the "La Linea" scandal. The prominence of US Ambassador Todd Robinson in the Guatemalan political scene once the scandal broke led to the suspicion that the US government was behind the investigation, perhaps because it needed an honest government in Guatemala to counter the presence of China and Russia in the region.<sup id="cite_ref-porras2015_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-porras2015-155">[155]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The UN anti-corruption committee has reported on other cases since then, and more than 20 government officials have stepped down. Some were arrested. Two of those cases involved two former presidential private secretaries: Juan de Dios Rodríguez in the Guatemalan Social Service and Gustave Martínez, who was involved in a bribery scandal at the coal power plant company. <a href="/w/index.php?title=Jaguar_Energy&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jaguar Energy (page does not exist)">Jaguar Energy</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&nbsp;[<a href="https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jaguar_Energy" class="extiw" title="es:Jaguar Energy">es</a>]</span> Martínez was also Perez Molina's son-in-law.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Leaders of the political opposition have also been implicated in CICIG investigations: several legislators and members of Libertad Democrática Renovada party (LIDER) were formally accused of bribery-related issues, prompting a large decline in the electoral prospects of its presidential candidate, Manuel Baldizón, who until April had been almost certain to become the next Guatemalan president in the 6 September 2015 presidential elections. Baldizón's popularity steeply declined and he filed accusations with the Organization of American States against CICIG leader Iván Velásquez of international obstruction in Guatemalan internal affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>CICIG reported its cases so often on Thursdays that Guatemalans coined the term "CICIG Thursdays". But a Friday press conference brought the crisis to its peak: on Friday 21 August 2015, the CICIG and Attorney General Thelma Aldana presented enough evidence to convince the public that both President Pérez Molina and former vice President Baldetti were the actual leaders of "La Línea". Baldetti was arrested the same day and an impeachment was requested for the president. Several cabinet members resigned and the clamor for the president's resignation grew after Perez Molina defiantly assured the nation in a televised message broadcast on 23 August 2015 that he was not going to resign.<sup id="cite_ref-periodico21a_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-periodico21a-158">[158]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-eu23a_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eu23a-159">[159]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Thousands of protesters took to the streets again, this time to demand the increasingly isolated president's resignation. Guatemala's Congress named a commission of five legislators to consider whether to remove the president's immunity from prosecution. The Supreme Court approved. A major day of action kicked off early on 27 August, with marches and roadblocks across the country. Urban groups who had spearheaded regular protests since the scandal broke in April, on the 27th sought to unite with the rural and indigenous organizations who orchestrated the road blocks. </p>
      <p>The strike in Guatemala City was full of a diverse and peaceful crowd ranging from the indigenous poor to the well-heeled, and it included many students from public and private universities. Hundreds of schools and businesses closed in support of the protests. The <i>Comité Coordinador de Asociaciones Agrícolas, Comerciales, Industriales y Financieras</i> (CACIF) Guatemala's most powerful business leaders, issued a statement demanding that Pérez Molina step down, and urged Congress to withdraw his immunity from prosecution.<sup id="cite_ref-cacifMolina_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cacifMolina-160">[160]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The attorney general's office released its own statement, calling for the president's resignation "to prevent ungovernability that could destabilize the nation." As pressure mounted, the president's former ministers of defence and of the interior, who had been named in the corruption investigation and resigned, abruptly left the country.<sup id="cite_ref-lopez27a_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lopez27a-161">[161]</a></sup> Pérez Molina meanwhile had been losing support by the day. The private sector called for his resignation; however, he also managed to get support from entrepreneurs that were not affiliated with the private sector chambers: Mario López Estrada – grandchild of former dictator <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Estrada_Cabrera" title="Manuel Estrada Cabrera">Manuel Estrada Cabrera</a> and the billionaire owner of cellular phone companies – had some of his executives assume the vacated cabinet positions.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Guatemalan radio station Emisoras Unidas reported exchanging text messages with Perez Molina. Asked whether he planned to resign, he wrote: "I will face whatever is necessary to face, and what the law requires." Some protesters demanded the general election be postponed, both because of the crisis and because it was plagued with accusations of irregularities. Others warned that suspending the vote could lead to an institutional vacuum.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> However, on 2 September 2015 Pérez Molina resigned, a day after Congress impeached him.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> On 3 September 2015 he was summoned to the Justice Department for his first legal audience for the <a href="/wiki/La_Linea_corruption_case" class="mw-redirect" title="La Linea corruption case">La Linea corruption case</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In June 2016 a United Nations-backed prosecutor described the administration of Pérez Molina as a crime syndicate and outlined another corruption case, this one dubbed <i>Cooperacha</i> (Kick-in). The head of the Social Security Institute and at least five other ministers pooled funds to buy him luxurious gifts such as motorboats, spending over $4.7 million in three years.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <table class="box-More_citations_needed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
       <tbody>
        <tr>
         <td class="mbox-image">
          <div style="width:52px">

      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Guatemala" title="Geography of Guatemala">Geography of Guatemala</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Un-guatemala.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Un-guatemala.png/220px-Un-guatemala.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="260" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Un-guatemala.png/330px-Un-guatemala.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Un-guatemala.png/440px-Un-guatemala.png 2x" data-file-width="2493" data-file-height="2947"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Un-guatemala.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A map of Guatemala.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Guatemala_K%C3%B6ppen.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Guatemala_K%C3%B6ppen.svg/220px-Guatemala_K%C3%B6ppen.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="298" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Guatemala_K%C3%B6ppen.svg/330px-Guatemala_K%C3%B6ppen.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Guatemala_K%C3%B6ppen.svg/440px-Guatemala_K%C3%B6ppen.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="765" data-file-height="1035"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Guatemala_K%C3%B6ppen.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Köppen climate types of Guatemala
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Quetzaltenango_farm_highlands_2009.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Quetzaltenango_farm_highlands_2009.jpg/220px-Quetzaltenango_farm_highlands_2009.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="94" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Quetzaltenango_farm_highlands_2009.jpg/330px-Quetzaltenango_farm_highlands_2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Quetzaltenango_farm_highlands_2009.jpg/440px-Quetzaltenango_farm_highlands_2009.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5669" data-file-height="2423"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Quetzaltenango_farm_highlands_2009.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The highlands of <a href="/wiki/Quetzaltenango" title="Quetzaltenango">Quetzaltenango</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Guatemala is mountainous with small patches of desert and sand dunes, all hilly valleys, except for the south coast and the vast northern lowlands of <a href="/wiki/Pet%C3%A9n_(department)" class="mw-redirect" title="Petén (department)">Petén</a> department. Two mountain chains enter Guatemala from west to east, dividing Guatemala into three major regions: the highlands, where the mountains are located; the Pacific coast, south of the mountains and the Petén region, north of the mountains. </p>
      <p>All major cities are located in the highlands and Pacific coast regions; by comparison, Petén is sparsely populated. These three regions vary in climate, elevation, and landscape, providing dramatic contrasts between hot, humid tropical lowlands and colder, drier highland peaks. <a href="/wiki/Volc%C3%A1n_Tajumulco" title="Volcán Tajumulco">Volcán Tajumulco</a>, at 4,220 metres (13,850 feet), is the highest point in the Central American countries. </p>
      <p>The rivers are short and shallow in the Pacific drainage basin, larger and deeper in the Caribbean and the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a> drainage basins. These rivers include the <a href="/wiki/Polochic_River" title="Polochic River">Polochic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dulce_River_(Guatemala)" title="Dulce River (Guatemala)">Dulce Rivers</a>, which drain into <a href="/wiki/Lake_Izabal" title="Lake Izabal">Lake Izabal</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Motagua_River" title="Motagua River">Motagua River</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sarstoon_River" title="Sarstoon River">Sarstún</a>, which forms the boundary with Belize, and the <a href="/wiki/Usumacinta_River" title="Usumacinta River">Usumacinta River</a>, which forms the boundary between Petén and <a href="/wiki/Chiapas" title="Chiapas">Chiapas</a>, Mexico. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_disasters">Natural disasters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Natural disasters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       For the 2018 volcanic eruption, see <a href="/wiki/2018_Volc%C3%A1n_de_Fuego_eruption" title="2018 Volcán de Fuego eruption">2018 Volcán de Fuego eruption</a>.
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Guatemala_town_volcanic_crater.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Guatemala_town_volcanic_crater.jpg/220px-Guatemala_town_volcanic_crater.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="97" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Guatemala_town_volcanic_crater.jpg/330px-Guatemala_town_volcanic_crater.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Guatemala_town_volcanic_crater.jpg/440px-Guatemala_town_volcanic_crater.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4288" data-file-height="1888"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Guatemala_town_volcanic_crater.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A town along the <a href="/wiki/Pan-American_Highway" title="Pan-American Highway">Pan-American Highway</a> within a volcanic crater.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Guatemala's location between the Caribbean Sea and Pacific Ocean makes it a target for hurricanes such as <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Mitch" title="Hurricane Mitch">Hurricane Mitch</a> in 1998 and <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Stan" title="Hurricane Stan">Hurricane Stan</a> in October 2005, which killed more than 1,500 people. The damage was not wind-related, but rather due to significant <a href="/wiki/Flooding" class="mw-redirect" title="Flooding">flooding</a> and resulting <a href="/wiki/Mudflow" title="Mudflow">mudslides</a>. The most recent was <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Eta" title="Hurricane Eta">Hurricane Eta</a> in November 2020, which was responsible for more than 100 missing or killed with the final tally still uncertain.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Guatemala's highlands lie along the <a href="/wiki/Motagua_Fault" title="Motagua Fault">Motagua Fault</a>, part of the boundary between the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Plate" title="Caribbean Plate">Caribbean</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_American_Plate" title="North American Plate">North American</a> <a href="/wiki/Tectonic_plates" class="mw-redirect" title="Tectonic plates">tectonic plates</a>. This fault has been responsible for several major earthquakes in historic times, including a 7.5 magnitude tremor on 4 February 1976 which killed more than 25,000 people. In addition, the <a href="/wiki/Middle_America_Trench" title="Middle America Trench">Middle America Trench</a>, a major <a href="/wiki/Subduction_zone" class="mw-redirect" title="Subduction zone">subduction zone</a> lies off the Pacific coast. Here, the <a href="/wiki/Cocos_Plate" title="Cocos Plate">Cocos Plate</a> is sinking beneath the Caribbean Plate, producing volcanic activity inland of the coast. Guatemala has 37 volcanoes, four of them active: <a href="/wiki/Pacaya" title="Pacaya">Pacaya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Santiaguito" class="mw-redirect" title="Santiaguito">Santiaguito</a>, <a href="/wiki/Volc%C3%A1n_de_Fuego" title="Volcán de Fuego">Fuego</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Volc%C3%A1n_Tacan%C3%A1" title="Volcán Tacaná">Tacaná</a>. </p>
      <p>Natural disasters have a long history in this geologically active part of the world. For example, two of the three <a href="/wiki/Antigua_Guatemala#History" title="Antigua Guatemala">moves</a> of the capital of Guatemala have been due to volcanic mudflows in 1541 and earthquakes in 1773. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiversity">Biodiversity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Biodiversity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Guatemala has 14 ecoregions ranging from mangrove forests to both ocean littorals with 5 different ecosystems. Guatemala has 252 listed wetlands, including five lakes, 61 lagoons, 100 rivers, and four swamps.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tikal" title="Tikal">Tikal</a> National Park was the first mixed UNESCO <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a>. Guatemala is a country of distinct <a href="/wiki/Fauna" title="Fauna">fauna</a>. It has some 1246 known species. Of these, 6.7% are <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">endemic</a> and 8.1% are threatened. Guatemala is home to at least 8,682 species of vascular plants, of which 13.5% are endemic. 5.4% of Guatemala is protected under IUCN categories I-V.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Maya_Biosphere_Reserve" title="Maya Biosphere Reserve">Maya Biosphere Reserve</a> in the department of <a href="/wiki/Pet%C3%A9n_(department)" class="mw-redirect" title="Petén (department)">Petén</a> has 2,112,940 ha,<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> making it the second-largest forest in Central America after <a href="/wiki/Bosawas" class="mw-redirect" title="Bosawas">Bosawas</a>. It had a 2019 <a href="/wiki/Forest_Landscape_Integrity_Index" title="Forest Landscape Integrity Index">Forest Landscape Integrity Index</a> mean score of 3.85/10, ranking it 138th globally out of 172 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-FLII-Supplementary_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FLII-Supplementary-171">[171]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_system">Political system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Political system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Guatemala" title="Politics of Guatemala">Politics of Guatemala</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:El_Congreso_de_la_Rep%C3%BAblica.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/El_Congreso_de_la_Rep%C3%BAblica.JPG/220px-El_Congreso_de_la_Rep%C3%BAblica.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/El_Congreso_de_la_Rep%C3%BAblica.JPG/330px-El_Congreso_de_la_Rep%C3%BAblica.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/El_Congreso_de_la_Rep%C3%BAblica.JPG/440px-El_Congreso_de_la_Rep%C3%BAblica.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:El_Congreso_de_la_Rep%C3%BAblica.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_Republic_of_Guatemala" title="Congress of the Republic of Guatemala">Congress of the Republic of Guatemala</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Guatemala is a constitutional democratic republic whereby the <a href="/wiki/President_of_Guatemala" title="President of Guatemala">President of Guatemala</a> is both <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">head of state</a> and <a href="/wiki/Head_of_government" title="Head of government">head of government</a>, and of a <a href="/wiki/Multi-party_system" title="Multi-party system">multi-party system</a>. <a href="/wiki/Executive_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Executive power">Executive power</a> is exercised by the government. <a href="/wiki/Legislative_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Legislative power">Legislative power</a> is vested in both the government and the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_Republic_of_Guatemala" title="Congress of the Republic of Guatemala">Congress of the Republic</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Judiciary" title="Judiciary">judiciary</a> is independent of the executive and the legislature. </p>
      <p>On 2 September 2015, <a href="/wiki/Otto_P%C3%A9rez_Molina" title="Otto Pérez Molina">Otto Pérez Molina</a> resigned as President of Guatemala due to <a href="/wiki/La_Linea_corruption_case" class="mw-redirect" title="La Linea corruption case">a corruption scandal</a> and was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Alejandro_Maldonado" title="Alejandro Maldonado">Alejandro Maldonado</a> until January 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-Maldonado_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maldonado-172">[172]</a></sup> Congress appointed former <a href="/wiki/Universidad_de_San_Carlos_de_Guatemala" title="Universidad de San Carlos de Guatemala">Universidad de San Carlos</a> President Alfonso Fuentes Soria as the new vice president to replace Maldonado.<sup id="cite_ref-soria_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-soria-173">[173]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Morales" title="Jimmy Morales">Jimmy Morales</a> assumed office on 14 January 2016. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">Foreign relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Foreign relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Guatemala" title="Foreign relations of Guatemala">Foreign relations of Guatemala</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Guatemala has long claimed all or part of the territory of neighboring Belize. Due to this territorial dispute, Guatemala did not recognize Belize's independence until 6 September 1991,<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> but the dispute is not resolved. Negotiations are currently under way under the auspices of the <a href="/wiki/Organization_of_American_States" title="Organization of American States">Organization of American States</a> to conclude it.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Military">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Military_of_Guatemala" class="mw-redirect" title="Military of Guatemala">Military of Guatemala</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Guatemala has a modest military, with between 15,000 and 20,000 personnel.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2017, Guatemala signed the United Nations <a href="/wiki/Treaty_on_the_Prohibition_of_Nuclear_Weapons" title="Treaty on the Prohibition of Nuclear Weapons">Treaty on the Prohibition of Nuclear Weapons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_divisions">Administrative divisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Administrative divisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Departments_of_Guatemala" title="Departments of Guatemala">Departments of Guatemala</a> and <a href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_Guatemala" title="Municipalities of Guatemala">Municipalities of Guatemala</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Guatemala is divided into 22 <a href="/wiki/Department_(subnational_entity)" class="mw-redirect" title="Department (subnational entity)">departments</a> (<a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>: <i>departamentos</i>) and sub-divided into about 335 municipalities (<a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>: <i>municipios</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-segeplan_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-segeplan-179">[179]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_rights">Human rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Human rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Violence_against_women_in_Guatemala" title="Violence against women in Guatemala">Violence against women in Guatemala</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guatemalan_Civil_War" title="Guatemalan Civil War">Guatemalan Civil War</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Killings and <a href="/wiki/Death_squad" title="Death squad">death squads</a> have been common in Guatemala since the end of the civil war in 1996. They often had ties to Clandestine Security Apparatuses (<i lang="es" title="Spanish-language text">Cuerpos Ilegales y Aparatos Clandestinos de Seguridad – CIACS</i>), organizations of current and former members of the military involved in organized crime. They had significant influence, now somewhat lessened,<sup id="cite_ref-ic_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ic-180">[180]</a></sup> but extrajudicial killings continue.<sup id="cite_ref-yale_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yale-181">[181]</a></sup> In July 2004, the <a href="/wiki/Inter-American_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="Inter-American Court">Inter-American Court</a> condemned the 18 July 1982 massacre of 188 Achi-Maya in Plan de Sanchez, and for the first time in its history, ruled the Guatemalan Army had committed genocide. It was the first ruling by the court against the Guatemalan state for any of the 626 massacres reported in its 1980s scorched-earth campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-yale_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yale-181">[181]</a></sup> In those massacres, 83 percent of the victims were Maya and 17 percent Ladino.<sup id="cite_ref-yale_181-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yale-181">[181]</a></sup> </p> 
      <table class="wikitable" style="align:center;"> 
       <tbody>
        <tr> 
         <th colspan="2">Extra-Judicial Killings in Guatemala </th>
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td style="text-align:center;">2010 </td> 
         <td style="text-align:right;">5,072 </td>
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td style="text-align:center;">2011 </td> 
         <td style="text-align:right;">279 </td>
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td style="text-align:center;">2012 </td> 
         <td style="text-align:right;">439 </td>
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td colspan="2">source: Center for Legal Action in Human Rights (CALDH)<sup id="cite_ref-ic_180-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ic-180">[180]</a></sup> </td>
        </tr> 
       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <p>In 2008, Guatemala became the first country to officially recognize <a href="/wiki/Femicide" title="Femicide">femicide</a>, the murder of a female because of her gender, as a crime.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-182">[182]</a></sup> Guatemala has the third-highest femicide rate in the world, after <a href="/wiki/El_Salvador" title="El Salvador">El Salvador</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaica</a>, with around 9.1 murders for every 100,000 women from 2007 to 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-182">[182]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Guatemala" title="Economy of Guatemala">Economy of Guatemala</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Guatemala_Export_Treemap.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Guatemala_Export_Treemap.jpg/220px-Guatemala_Export_Treemap.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Guatemala_Export_Treemap.jpg/330px-Guatemala_Export_Treemap.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Guatemala_Export_Treemap.jpg/440px-Guatemala_Export_Treemap.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2668" data-file-height="2085"></a>

      <div class="thumb tright">

        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">

        </div>

      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Dole_Ecuador_(3746433687).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Dole_Ecuador_%283746433687%29.jpg/220px-Dole_Ecuador_%283746433687%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Dole_Ecuador_%283746433687%29.jpg/330px-Dole_Ecuador_%283746433687%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Dole_Ecuador_%283746433687%29.jpg/440px-Dole_Ecuador_%283746433687%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3361" data-file-height="1898"></a>

       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Guatemala is the largest economy in Central America, with a GDP (PPP) per capita of US$5,200. However, Guatemala faces many social problems and is one of the poorest countries in Latin America. The income distribution is highly unequal with more than half of the population below the national poverty line and just over 400,000 (3.2%) unemployed. The CIA World Fact Book considers 54.0% of the population of Guatemala to be living in poverty in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-183">[183]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2010, the <a href="/wiki/Guatemalan_economy" class="mw-redirect" title="Guatemalan economy">Guatemalan economy</a> grew by 3%, recovering gradually from the 2009 crisis, as a result of the falling demands from the United States and other <a href="/wiki/Central_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Central American">Central American</a> markets and the slowdown in foreign investment in the middle of the <a href="/wiki/Global_recession_of_2009" class="mw-redirect" title="Global recession of 2009">global recession</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Remittance" title="Remittance">Remittances</a> from Guatemalans living in United States now constitute the largest single source of foreign income (two-thirds of exports and one tenth of GDP).<sup id="cite_ref-cia_183-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-183">[183]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Some of Guatemala's main exports are fruits, vegetables, flowers, handicrafts, cloths and others. In the face of a rising demand for <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a>, the country is growing and exporting an increasing amount of raw materials for biofuel production, especially <a href="/wiki/Sugar_cane" class="mw-redirect" title="Sugar cane">sugar cane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Palm_oil" title="Palm oil">palm oil</a>. Critics say that this development leads to higher prices for <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">staple foods</a> like corn, a major ingredient in the Guatemalan diet. As a consequence of the subsidization of US American corn, Guatemala imports nearly half of its corn from the United States that is using 40 percent of its crop harvest for biofuel production.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> In 2014, the government was considering ways to legalize poppy and marijuana production, hoping to tax production and use tax revenues to fund drug prevention programs and other social projects.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Gross Domestic Product (GDP) in <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">purchasing power parity</a> (PPP) in 2010 was estimated at US$70.15 billion. The service sector is the largest component of GDP at 63%, followed by the industry sector at 23.8% and the agriculture sector at 13.2% (2010 est.). Mines produce gold, silver, zinc, cobalt and nickel.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> The agricultural sector accounts for about two-fifths of exports, and half of the labor force. Organic coffee, sugar, textiles, fresh vegetables, and bananas are the country's main exports. Inflation was 3.9% in 2010. </p>
      <p>The 1996 peace accords that ended the decades-long civil war removed a major obstacle to foreign investment. Tourism has become an increasing source of revenue for Guatemala thanks to the new foreign investment. </p>
      <p>In March 2006, Guatemala's congress ratified the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic-Central_America_Free_Trade_Agreement" class="mw-redirect" title="Dominican Republic-Central America Free Trade Agreement">Dominican Republic&nbsp;– Central American Free Trade Agreement (DR-CAFTA)</a> between several Central American nations and the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> Guatemala also has <a href="/wiki/Free_trade_agreement" title="Free trade agreement">free trade agreements</a> with <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Tourism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Guatemala" title="Tourism in Guatemala">Tourism in Guatemala</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Tourism has become one of the main drivers of the economy, with tourism estimated at $1.8 billion to the economy in 2008. Guatemala receives around two million tourists annually. In recent years, there has been an increase in the number of cruise ships visiting Guatemalan seaports, leading to higher tourist numbers. Tourist destinations include Mayan archaeological sites (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Tikal" title="Tikal">Tikal</a> in the Peten, Quiriguá in Izabal, Iximche in Tecpan Chimaltenango and <a href="/wiki/Guatemala_City" title="Guatemala City">Guatemala City</a>), natural attractions (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Lake_Atitl%C3%A1n" title="Lake Atitlán">Lake Atitlán</a> and <a href="/wiki/Semuc_Champey" title="Semuc Champey">Semuc Champey</a>) and historical sites such as the colonial city of <a href="/wiki/Antigua_Guatemala" title="Antigua Guatemala">Antigua Guatemala</a>, which is recognized as a UNESCO Cultural Heritage site. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society">Society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guatemala&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

      <p>Guatemala has a population of 17,247,849 (2018 est).<sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_2019_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP_2019-8">[8]</a></sup> With only 885,000 in 1900, this constitutes the fastest population growth in the Western Hemisphere during the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> The Republic of Guatemala's first census was taken in 1778,<sup id="cite_ref-:0_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-192">[192]</a></sup> and despite the fact that these censuses have been fairly accurate, they have not been taken at regular intervals. The 1940 census was burned.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-193">[193]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> Data from the remaining censuses is in the Historical Population table below. </p>
